==PAGE:1==
 Master Thesis 
Ecotoxicity of bioplastic leachates 
to Daphnia magna 
Asta Gøttsche (s194448) 
 
 
Technical University of Denmark 
DTU Sustain 
June 12th, 2024 
 
==PAGE:2==
 
2
Abstract 
Since the beginning of mass production of plastics in the 1950s, it is estimated as of 2015 that virgin 
plastics have accumulated to 8,300 million metric tonnes (Mt) [Geyer et al., 2017]. Annually, plastics 
are produced globally of approximately 400 Mt as of 2022 and increasing at an annual rate of 4 % 
[PlasticsEurope, 2023; Geyer et al., 2017]. Today, bioplastics represent roughly 0.5 % of annual 
plastic production worldwide, equivalent to around 2.18 Mt as of 2023 [PlasticsEurope, 2023; 
European Bioplastics, 2023]. Approximately 48 % of the 2.18 Mt of bioplastics represent biobased 
non-biodegradable plastics, while the other 52% represent biodegradable plastics. [European 
Bioplastics, 2023].  
Bioplastic is the newest member of the plastic family, the flower child of the family, which offers a 
promising environmentally friendly alternative to the fossil-based plastics family. Contrary to 
conventional plastic, bioplastic is either made biodegradable (fossil-made), biobased (biomass-
made), or both [European Bioplastics, 2024]. 
The main aim of the thesis is to investigate the ecotoxicity of bioplastic bags on the test organism 
freshwater zooplankton Daphnia magna. Three bioplastic and two conventional plastic bags were 
applied in the thesis: the shopping bag, the food-waste bag, the bio-waste bag, the conventional waste 
bag, and the grocery carrier bag. Two acute toxicity tests were conducted with 10- and 20-day water-
extraction leaching of plastics and bioplastics, and one acute toxicity test was conducted with 
methanol/DMSO-extraction. The acute toxicity tests followed the OECD guideline no. 202. 
Combined with the acute tests, the toxicity identification evaluations were applied to evaluate if the 
toxicant in leachates could be found. Additionally, one chronic toxicity test was conducted on two 
bioplastics: the shopping bag and the bio-waste bag The chronic toxicity tests followed the OECD 
guideline no. 202. Analyses of metals, carbon, nitrogen, and the number of chemical features were 
performed. 
On the basis of the results obtained and analyses, the bioplastics, the shopping bag and the bio-waste 
bag may pose an ecotoxicological effect on aquatic environments if they were wrongfully disposed 
of, as these two bags had the most significant impact on D. magna compared to other bags. The 
shopping bag leachate had a 48-hour EC50 of 76 %, while the bio-waste bag leachate had a 48-hour 
EC50 of 68 %. The other bags, the food-waste bag, the conventional waste bag, and the grocery carrier 
bag, had minimal to no acute toxicity on D. magna. Which toxicant that did contribute to the toxicity 
in the bioplastic leachates is still unclear, as the toxicity identification evaluation results were unclear. 
The toxicant identified in the shopping bag, which may contribute to the acute toxicity on D. magna, 
could be metal and organic chemicals. In contrast, the toxicant that may contribute to chronic toxicity 
remains unclear, as no TIEs were applied, so the microplastics, metals, and organic chemicals could 
contribute to the toxicity. The toxicity in the leachate reduced the survival of parent D. magna, caused 
a delay in molting, a higher average cumulative offspring per parent in all concentrations, and a 
reduced average length of offspring in the 6.25 and 25 % leachates. 
The toxicant identified in the bio-waste bag, which may have contributed to the acute toxicity on D. 
magna, is unclear, but it could be either organic chemicals or microplastics. Moreover, the toxicant 
that may have contributed to chronic toxicity also remains unclear. The conclusion of the chronic 
==PAGE:3==
 
3
toxicity of the shopping bag also applies to the bio-waste bag. Furthermore, the toxicity in the leachate 
reduced the survival of parent D. magna, caused a delay in molting, and a reduced average length of 
offspring in the 25 % leachates. 
Little is still known about the fate and behavior of the bioplastic in freshwater environments, as well 
as other environmental compartments. Also, more is needed to know about the toxicity that bioplastic 
leachates may pose to aquatic environments, as the studies addressing the effects of bioplastic 
leachates on freshwater species are limited. Therefore, further efforts are necessary to clarify the 
extent of the influence of the additives leached from bioplastics. 
 
Sammenfatning 
Siden begyndelsen af masseproduktionen af plastik i 1950'erne anslås det fra 2015, at jomfruplast er 
akkumuleret til 8.300 millioner tons (Mt) [Geyer et al., 2017]. På verdensplan produceres der årligt 
plastik på ca. 400 Mt fra 2022, og det stiger med en årlig rate på 4 % [PlasticsEurope, 2023; Geyer et 
al., 2017]. I dag repræsenterer bioplast omkring 0,5 % af den årlige plastproduktion på verdensplan, 
svarende til omkring 2,18 Mt i 2023 [PlasticsEurope, 2023; European Bioplastics, 2023]. Cirka 48 % 
af de 2,18 Mt bioplast repræsenterer biobaseret, ikke-bionedbrydelig plast, mens de øvrige 52 % 
repræsenterer bionedbrydelig plast. [European Bioplastics, 2023]. 
Bioplast er det nyeste medlem af plastikfamilien, familiens blomsterbarn, som tilbyder et lovende 
miljøvenligt alternativ til den fossilbaserede plastikfamilie. I modsætning til konventionel plast er 
bioplast enten gjort bionedbrydeligt (fossilfremstillet), biobaseret (biomasse-fremstillet) eller begge 
dele [European Bioplastics, 2024]. 
Hovedformålet med afhandlingen er at undersøge økotoksiciteten af bioplastposer på testorganismen 
ferskvands zooplankton Daphnia magna. Tre bioplastposer og to konventionelle plastikposer blev 
anvendt i specialet: en indkøbspose (shopping bag), en madaffaldspose (food-waste bag), en 
bioaffaldspose (bio-waste bag), en konventionel affaldspose (conventional waste bag) og en 
indkøbsbærepose (grocery carrier bag). To akut toksicitetstests blev udført med 10- og 20-dages 
vandekstrakteret perkolater af plast og bioplast, og en akut toksicitetstest blev udført med 
metanol/DMSO-ekstrakt. De akutte toksicitetstests fulgte OECD's retningslinjer nr. 202. Kombineret 
med de akutte test blev toksicitetsidentifikationsevalueringerne anvendt til at vurdere, om toksinet i 
perkolater kunne findes. Derudover blev der udført en kronisk toksicitetstest på to bioplast: 
indkøbsposen (shopping bag) og bioaffaldsposen (bio-waste bag). De kroniske toksicitetstests fulgte 
OECD's retningslinjer nr. 202. Der blev udført analyser af metaller, kulstof, nitrogen og antallet af 
kemiske features. 
På baggrund af de opnåede resultater og analyser kan bioplasterne, indkøbsposen (shopping bag) og 
bioaffaldsposen (bio-waste bag), have en økotoksikologisk effekt på akvatiske miljøer, hvis de blev 
fejlagtig bortskaffet, da disse to poser havde den største påvirkning på D. magna sammenlignet med 
de andre poser. Perkolat fra indkøbsposen (shopping bag) havde en 48-timers EC50 på 76 %, mens 
bioaffaldsposen (bio-waste bag) havde en 48-timers EC50 på 68 %. De andre poser, madaffaldsposen 
(food-waste bag), konventionel affaldsposen (conventional waste bag) og indkøbsbæreposen (grocery 
==PAGE:4==
 
4
carrier bag), havde minimal eller ingen akut toksicitet på D. magna. Hvilket toksin, der bidrog til 
toksiciteten i bioplastperkolat, er stadig uklart, da resultaterne af toksicitetsidentifikations-
evalueringerne var uklare. 
Toksinet identificeret i indkøbsposen (shopping bag), som bidrog til akut toksicitet på D. magna, kan 
være metal og organiske kemikalier. Hvorimod, toksinet som bidrog til kronisk toksicitet, er uklart, 
da der ikke blev anvendt TIEs, så mikroplast, metaller og organiske kemikalier kan bidrage til 
toksiciteten. Perkolattoksiciteten reducerede overlevelsen af forælder D. magna, forårsagede en 
forsinkelse i yngletid, et højere gennemsnitligt kumulativt afkom pr. forælder i alle koncentrationer 
og en reduceret gennemsnitlig længde af afkom i 6,25 og 25 % perkolat. 
Toksinet identificeret i bioaffaldsposen (bio-waste bag), som kan have bidraget til akut toksicitet på 
D. magna, er uklart, men det kan enten være organiske kemikalier eller mikroplast. Desuden er det 
toksinet, der kan have bidraget til kronisk toksicitet, også uklart. Konklusionen på indkøbsposens 
(shopping bag) kroniske toksicitet gælder også for bioaffaldsposen (bio-waste bag). Desuden 
reducerede perkolattoksiciteten overlevelsen af forælder D. magna, forårsagede en forsinkelse i 
yngletid og en reduceret gennemsnitlig længde af afkom i 25 % perkolat. 
Der vides stadig lidt om bioplastens skæbne og opførelse i ferskvandsmiljøer såvel som andre miljøer. 
Der er også behov for at vide mere om den toksicitet, som bioplastperkolater kan påvirke i 
vandmiljøer, da forskning om påvirkningerne af bioplastperkolater på ferskvandsarter, er 
begrænsede. Derfor er der behov for en yderligere indsats for at klarlægge omfanget af påvirkningen 
af de tilsætningsstoffer, der udvaskes fra bioplast.

Preface 
This Master thesis and its defense account for 35 ECTS, and it was prepared in collaboration with the 
Department of Environmental and Resource Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark 
(DTU). The thesis fulfills the requirements for acquiring a degree in Master of Science in Engineering 
(cand.polyt.) in Environmental Engineering. The thesis was prepared from January 1st, 2024 to June 
12th, 2024. The thesis was accomplished with the supervisor Senior Researcher Nanna B. Hartmann 
and co-supervisor Ph.D. student Sevil Vafadar Afshar. The thesis is titled “Ecotoxicity of bioplastic 
leachates to Daphnia magna” (dan: Økotoksicitet af bioplastperkolater i Daphnia magna). 
The AI writing program Grammarly has been used in this thesis. The program is exclusively used for 
grammar checking and wording. The program has not been used to generate texts or responses in 
relation to obtaining the objectives of the thesis. 
Note: throughout the thesis, the decimals are separated by periods, and commas separate the thousand 
separators. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Kongens Lyngby, University of Denmark, June 12th, 2024

1 Introduction 
Since the beginning of mass production of plastics in the 1950s, it is estimated as of 2015 that virgin 
plastics have accumulated to 8,300 million metric tonnes (Mt) [Geyer et al., 2017]. Annually, plastics 
are produced globally of approximately 400 Mt as of 2022 and increasing at an annual rate of 4 % 
[PlasticsEurope, 2023; Geyer et al., 2017]. In the big picture, plastic waste has three different fates: 
reusing/recycling, incineration, or discarded in landfills or improperly disposed of in nature [Geyer 
et al., 2017]. With the Geyer et al. (2017) model, projections show that the fates of the total 
accumulated plastics ever made are distributed as follows: 12 % is in currently use, while 9 % is 
recycled (thereof, only 10% have been recycled more than once), 12 % is incinerated, and the residual 
(60 %) is landfilled or improperly disposed of. About 70% of the plastic placed on the market becomes 
waste in the same year [Wagner et al., 2024]. When plastics are improperly disposed of in the natural 
environment, degradation can take hundreds to thousands of years; however, some petrochemical 
plastics can biodegrade in nature in a few months to a few years because of their physicochemical 
properties [Atiwesh et al., 2021]. 
Bioplastic is the newest member of the plastic family, the flower child of the family, which offers a 
promising environmentally friendly alternative to the fossil-based plastics family. Contrary to 
conventional plastic, bioplastic is either made biodegradable (fossil-made), biobased (biomass-
made), or both [European Bioplastics, 2024]. Today, bioplastics represent roughly 0.5 % of annual 
plastic production worldwide, equivalent to around 2.18 Mt as of 2023 [PlasticsEurope, 2023; 
European Bioplastics, 2023]. Approximately 48 % of the 2.18 Mt of bioplastics represent biobased 
non-biodegradable plastics, while the other 52% represent biodegradable plastics. [European 
Bioplastics, 2023].  
Additionally, studies have shown that the average everyday plastic consumer does not sort and recycle 
bioplastic correctly [Ansink et al., 2021; Dilkes-Hoffman et al., 2019]. Consumers are not informed 
sufficiently about all the different types of bioplastics and how to recycle them in an environmentally 
friendly way; thus, some of the bioplastics might end up as residual waste instead of recycling and 
vice versa [Ansink et al., 2021; Dilkes-Hoffman et al., 2019]. With the promise that bioplastics easily 
degrade in nature, the current littering becomes a more significant problem due to the shifting of 
responsibilities from the individuals, thinking that the “biodegrading” bioplastic will disappear in 
nature [Haider et al., 2019]. 
Similarly to conventional plastics, bioplastics have chemicals and additives added to improve their 
functionality and mechanical properties, which can harm the environment. In a study by Zimmermann 
et al. (2020), 80 % of 33 bioplastic products encompassed more than 1000 unknown chemical 
features, and 67 % encompassed toxic chemical features. Moreover, by comparing conventional 
plastics with bioplastics, Zimmermann et al. (2020a) concluded that bioplastics are similarly toxic. 
Information regarding chemicals and additives in bioplastic products is opaque [Zimmermann et al., 
2019 and 2020a]. This indicates that it is hard to determine the chemical compositions of the 
bioplastics and, thus, whether the bioplastic is toxic. Therefore, assessing their toxicity is crucial. 
When bioplastics end up in an aquatic environment, there may be a risk of bioplastic leaching 
unknown and toxic components and thus contaminating water bodies. Leachate is generated when a 
==PAGE:13==
 
13
material is in contact with a solvent, e.g., water, which can release chemicals matrixed in the 
materials, e.g., additives [Cambridge Dictionary, 2024]. If these chemicals and additives are released 
into freshwater, there is a probability that freshwater organisms, animals, and humans can be affected 
by drinking the toxic water. Therefore, the effect of the bioplastic leachates on the freshwater 
environment is of great relevance. The preliminary literature research suggests that few to no 
ecotoxicity investigations of bioplastic leachates have been conducted [SAPEA, 2020; Afshar et al., 
2024; section 2.4.2]. The literature also indicates that further ecotoxicity tests on bioplastic leachates 
are relevant, especially since the endpoint of leachates is often in freshwater environments [Gunaalan 
et al., 2020; Emadian et al., 2017]. 
The acute and chronic ecotoxicity effects of three bioplastics and two conventional plastics are 
assessed in this thesis. The impacts of bioplastic and conventional plastic leachates are compared in 
the freshwater environment since they are essential as freshwater is used as drinking water. 
Accordingly, freshwater zooplankton species Daphnia magna is applied in acute and chronic toxicity 
experiments to investigate the ecotoxicity effects of bioplastic and conventional plastic leachates. 
Moreover, the toxicity identification evaluation (TIE) approach from the whole effluent toxicity 
(WET) method on wastewater monitoring is also applied. The TIE method is applied to narrow down 
which components in bioplastic and conventional plastic leachates are toxic. The method includes 
physicochemical manipulation of bioplastic leachates, which is applied to test whether the toxic 
components in bioplastics are metals or organic chemicals. 
In further assessing the conventional plastic and bioplastics leachates, metal, carbon, and nitrogen 
content is analyzed, and a non-targeting analysis is conducted. 
1.1 The aim, objectives, and boundaries of the thesis 
The aim of this thesis is to investigate the ecotoxicity of bioplastic bags, which are used for shopping, 
food waste, and general waste, where the water-extracted leachates from these are tested on 
freshwater zooplankton Daphnia magna. Conventional plastic bags used for general waste and 
grocery shopping are also applied in the toxicity test. An additional toxicity test is conducted on 
methanol-extracted plastic and bioplastic bags to compare the leached and extracted content of the 
bags. To reach the aim of the thesis, the objectives are to: 
- 
identify bioplastics and what challenges they may pose 
- 
create an overview of current knowledge of ecotoxicities of bioplastic leachates 
- 
assess the ecotoxicological effect of plastic and bioplastic leachates on D. magna 
- 
evaluate and find knowledge gaps on the ecotoxicological impact of the leachates and if the 
leachate toxicity can be attributed to metals, organic chemicals, or microplastics 
To obtain the objectives, the following laboratory experiments were performed: 
- 
acute ecotoxicity tests, which are constructed around the OECD guide no. 202, with 10- and 
20-day water-extraction leaching of plastics and bioplastics 
- 
acute ecotoxicity test with methanol/DMSO-extraction of plastics and bioplastics 
- 
toxicity identification evaluations combined with acute ecotoxicity tests: targeting metals 
and organic chemicals by using activated carbon and EDTA, as well as non-filtration 
manipulation 
==PAGE:14==
 
14
- 
chronic ecotoxicity test, which is constructed around the OECD guide no. 211, with 20-day 
water-extraction leaching of two bioplastics 
- 
analyses of concentrations of metals, carbon content, and non-targeting 
The boundary of the thesis follows: 
- 
only two plastics and three bioplastics are applied in the acute ecotoxicity tests 
- 
only three physical-chemical manipulations are used in the toxicity identification evaluations 
- 
only two bioplastics were applied in the chronic ecotoxicity tests 
- 
systematic literature research is not conducted, but instead, databases (DTU Findit and Web 
of Science) are used to find scientific literature to obtain basic knowledge and an overview 
and perspective of where the current knowledge about the ecotoxicity of bioplastic leachates 
stands 
An overview of the time plans of the thesis period can be found in Appendix M. 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:15==
 
15
2 Theory 
In the section "Theory", general knowledge is provided to understand and read the “Methods and 
Procedures”, “Results”, and “Discussion” sections of the thesis. Knowledge about: 
- 
bioplastics, and the challenges of the bioplastics, 
- 
Daphnia magna ecotoxicity tests and the test organism Daphnia magna, 
- 
the effect concentrations (ECX) and concentration-response curves, 
- 
ecotoxicity of complex leachates, 
- 
whole effluent toxicity and toxicity identification evaluation, 
- 
metals and organic chemicals in plastics and bioplastics and leachates thereof, 
- 
existing examples of ecotoxicity tests on bioplastic leachates, and 
- 
literature research 
are described in this section. 
2.1 Bioplastics 
Plastics without additional chemicals are not plastic [Wagner et al., 2024]. Plastic itself consists of 
monomers that are polymerized with starting substances to produce polymers, which form the 
backbone of a plastic material [Wagner et al., 2024]. If no further chemicals were added to plastics, 
most plastics would be unstable in the environment [Wagner et al., 2024]. For example, without 
stabilizing chemicals, plastics would easily degrade and release toxic components if exposed to 
sunlight or heat [Wagner et al., 2024]. Furthermore, without plasticizers, plastics are not moldable 
and flexible [Wagner et al., 2024]. Other additives are added to achieve the desired properties of 
plastic products [Wagner et al., 2024]. These additional chemicals are also in bioplastics. 
 
Figure 1: Classification of bioplastics. Bioplastics can either be biobased and non-biodegradable (up to the left), biobased and 
biodegradable (up to the right), fossil-based and biodegradable (down to the right), or fossil-based and non-biodegradable 
(conventional plastics) (down to the left). Source: [European Bioplastics, 2024]. 
The term “bioplastics” defines plastics produced from biomass and processed into finished products 
with additional chemicals [Lambert & Wagner, 2017]. This does not denote that the bioplastics are 
==PAGE:16==
 
16
biodegradable. The term also represents biodegradable fossil-based plastics, but they need a certain 
degree of biodegradability to be deemed bioplastic [European Bioplastics, 2024; Directive 2019/904]. 
Bioplastics are classified into three groups, depending on whether they are biobased and their 
biodegradability (Figure 1) [European Bioplastics, 2024]. Bioplastics can be biobased and non-
biodegradable, fossil-based and biodegradable, or biobased and biodegradable. The fossil-based and 
non-biodegradable plastics are known as conventional plastics. Plastics that undergo biodegradation 
are considered biodegradable if they can be biodegraded by microorganisms such as bacteria, fungi, 
and algae, resulting in the conversion of plastic carbon to CO2 (and CH4 in anaerobe condition), water, 
mineral salts, and microbial biomass [Lackner et al., 2023; Mastrolia et al., 2022]. 
Biobased plastics originate or partly originate from renewable biomass such as plants, animals, 
microbial sources, and organic waste [Lackner et al., 2023; Atiwesh et al., 2021; European 
Bioplastics, 2024; SAPEA, 2020]. Biobased plastics can be non-biodegradable or biodegradable 
depending on the chemical structure of the plastic [Atiwesh et al., 2021]. The non-biodegradable 
biobased plastics are called “drop-ins”. The drop-in bioplastics are chemically identical to and use 
the same pathway as their fossil equivalents, but they are made with biomass instead of 
petrochemicals [Wageningen University, 2024]. Moreover, these bioplastics are non-biodegradable 
as they have inherited the same structure and properties [Atiwesh et al., 2021]. 
Biobased and biodegradable plastics are primarily produced from starch blends such as corn and 
sugarcane [Atiwesh et al., 2021]. This type of bioplastic is readily biodegradable, e.g., by hydrolysis 
[Atiwesh et al., 2021]. 
Petrochemical plastics are fossil-based, e.g., oil and natural gas, but some plastics can biodegrade. 
Their physicochemical properties allow them to biodegrade; thus, they are considered bioplastic 
[Atiwesh et al., 2021]. 
2.1.1 The challenges of the bioplastics 
As pointed out in the “Introduction” section, we were promised that bioplastics would solve our great 
plastic problem and that all bioplastics are universally good. The word “bioplastic” deceives the 
average everyday bioplastic consumer and is used interchangeably, implying that all bioplastics are 
biobased and can biodegrade in all environments [Lambert & Wagner, 2017]. Bioplastics are often 
considered environmentally friendly, less persistent, and less toxic than conventional petrochemical 
plastic [Lambert & Wagner, 2017]. This is inaccurate. 
To biodegrade, or not to biodegrade? 
As mentioned in section 2.1, bioplastics can both be biodegradable or non-biodegradable due to their 
biodegradability: the potential for a bioplastic to biodegrade [Lambert & Wagner, 2017; Afshar et al., 
2024]. Moreover, the biodegradability of a bioplastic depends on its chemical structure, which 
contains the carbon backbone and the additional chemicals that hold, form, and/or give a degradation 
mechanism [Lambert & Wagner, 2017]. Thus, the chemicals can either enhance or diminish the 
biodegradability of a bioplastic [Lambert & Wagner, 2017; Emadian et al., 2017]. So, both the 
==PAGE:17==
 
17
biodegradability of the final bioplastic product and the environmental conditions govern the degree 
of biodegradation [Haider et al., 2019; Napper & Thompson, 2019]. 
The conditions in open environments (aquatic, terrestrial, or marine) vary throughout the season and 
the ecological settings [Haider et al., 2019; Napper & Thompson, 2019], which influence the 
biological activities, such as variations in enzymes, microorganisms, temperature, pH, humidity, 
oxygen supply, and light, that cause biodegradation of bioplastics. [Haider et al., 2019; Emadian et 
al., 2017]. These factors, together with the biodegradability of a bioplastic, determine the timeframe 
(months to years) for a complete biodegradation [Haider et al., 2019; Napper & Thompson, 2019; 
Chamas et al., 2020]. 
A bioplastic wrongly disposed of will interact with the open environments in several different states 
throughout its lifetime, as its characteristics (size, weight, structure, and surface properties) can be 
modified by environmental conditions and weathering [Chamas et al., 2020; Napper & Thompson, 
2019; Bejgarn et al., 2015]. In the end, many conditions and factors influence the biodegradation of 
bioplastics and thus make it difficult to determine the exact biodegradation rate and the 
biodegradability thereof. Consequently, the biodegradation assessments of bioplastics must be 
specific to the open environment, as the conditions can be in broad and complex range. 
Today, several standardized test procedures have been developed to determine the biodegradation of 
bioplastics and allow for comparing the biodegradations across studies [SAPEA, 2020; Afshar et al., 
2024]. However, not all studies follow standardized test procedures, and the available standardized 
tests today do not exist for all open environmental compartments [Afshar et al., 2024]. In a systematic 
literature review, Afshar et al. (2024) found that only 9.2 % and 3.1 % followed a standard or a 
modified standard test method of degradation of biodegradable plastics, respectively, while the 
remaining 88 % did not follow any standard test methods. The soil was the leading standardized test 
environment, accounting for 73 % of the tests. At the same time, standardized tests for freshwater 
ecosystems (lakes, streams, and rivers) are absent, which is the middle ground of bioplastic littering 
[Afshar et al., 2024; Gunaalan et al., 2020; Emadian et al., 2017]. This suggests that it is hard to 
standardize tests that can be comparable in all environmental compartments, even in research on 
biodegradation. Standardizing tests on plastics and bioplastics combined with, e.g., persistence and 
toxicity tests across all environmental compartments is necessary to avoid unwanted accumulation 
and toxicity in the open environment [Lambert & Wagner, 2017; Gunaalan et al., 2020].  
Microplastics originating from bioplastics 
The current standardized tests for assessing the biodegradability of materials of different shapes and 
sizes are also absent despite being recognized as essential [Harrison et al., 2018]. Materials with a 
high surface area to volume ratio, such as microplastics, may exhibit higher rates of biodegradation, 
but the microplastics are easily transported between environments, so biodegradation rates are 
difficult to determine [Lambert & Wagner, 2017; Harrison et al., 2018]. 
Also, compared to conventional plastics, biodegradable bioplastics may be more easily degraded into 
microplastics, and the fate and timing of the microplastics originating from bioplastics are mostly 
unknown, thus making them potentially as harmful as microplastics originating from conventional 
==PAGE:18==
 
18
plastics [Emadian et al., 2017]. Some bioplastics intended for biodegradation by composting, e.g., 
poly(lactic acid) (PLA), if not fully biodegraded in the open environment, may contribute to 
microplastic debris, but, again, it is dependent on their biodegradation rate in the receiving 
environments [Lambert & Wagner, 2017].  
Determining whether the additives or the bioplastics microscopic fragments are toxic to the species 
that ingest fragments, such as deposit- and filter-feeding invertebrates (primary consumers in the 
aquatic food webs), can be challenging. Additives can be toxic to them, but microplastics can also 
harm them by causing system obstruction [Ali et al., 2024; Harrison et al., 2018]. The ingested 
bioplastic fragments may also leach additives inside the species, but their hazardous effects after 
ingestion may be less in organisms able to biodegrade the fragments [Lambert & Wagner, 2017]. At 
this point, the assumptions are based on a limited number of studies [Lambert & Wagner, 2017; 
SAPEA, 2020]. In one of the studies, microplastics originating from bioplastic PLA were tested on 
zooplankton Daphnia magna in a chronic toxicity test, which increased mortality to 60% and reduced 
the mean body length of parent D. magna [Zimmermann et al., 2020b]. They concluded that the 
effects on D. magna were induced by the microplastic particles and not by the extracted or leached 
additives. 
Adsorption of organic and inorganic pollutants 
The adverse effects of bioplastics can intensify when they coexist with other environmental pollutants 
[Ribba et al., 2022]. Other pollutants could be heavy metals, including copper and lead, and organic 
chemicals, such as persistent organic pollutants (POPs), which can interact with bioplastics by 
adsorbing onto them [Bejgarn et al., 2015; Balestri et al., 2019; Ribba et al., 2022]. The type and 
number of pollutants bioplastics can adsorb depends on the physical and chemical properties of the 
polymer, such as hydrophobicity and surface area [Rochman et al., 2019]. Recent research has shown 
that different types of pollutants adsorbed on bioplastics and plastics could alter not only the behavior 
and toxicity of themselves but also the co-existing pollutants [Ali et al., 2024]. However, the 
combined effects of biodegradation of bioplastics and co-existing pollutants are scarce, as most 
studies have focused on conventional plastics [SAPEA, 2020; Ali et al., 2024]. 
Challenges associated with bioplastics recycling and littering 
Inadequate waste and recycling management across the globe causes the accumulation of plastic in 
the environment [Haider et al., 2019]. With the introduction of bioplastics, it is feared that consumers 
might overestimate the biodegradation time and continue to litter [Haider et al., 2019]. Today, 
everyday consumers of bioplastics do not sort and recycle bioplastic correctly [Ansink et al., 2021; 
Dilkes-Hoffman et al., 2019]. Consumers are not sufficiently informed about all the man different 
types of bioplastics and how to recycle and dispose of them in an environmentally friendly way, and, 
thus, some of the bioplastics may end up as residual waste instead of recycling and vice versa [Ansink 
et al., 2021; Dilkes-Hoffman et al., 2019]. The additional problem is that “biodegradable” could be 
used in the name of the material without any actual evidence of its biodegradability [Haider et al., 
2019; SAPEA, 2020].  
==PAGE:19==
 
19
With the promise that bioplastics biodegrade and disappear when ending up in the open environment, 
current littering becomes a more substantial problem due to the shifting of responsibilities from the 
consumers thinking that the bioplastic will disappear in nature [Haider et al., 2019; Zimmermann et 
al., 2020b]. Consumers also perceive the biodegradability of bioplastics as an excuse for incorrect 
waste handling [Haider et al., 2019]. If bioplastics persist in the open environment, they may be 
considered as additional organic carbon input to the open environment, and the risks will likely be 
the same as those of conventional plastics [Harrison et al., 2018; SAPEA, 2020]. 
Accordingly, if bioplastics are not correctly managed and disposed of, and, thus, can enter the open 
environment, environmental issues may occur, e.g., bioplastics leach chemicals and release 
microplastics. Bioplastic litter can be both accidental and intentional (e.g., agricultural mulch films). 
It is crucial to differentiate between bioplastics designed to be composed and those intended to 
biodegrade in the open environment [SAPEA, 2020]. Those bioplastics designed as compostable can 
undergo decomposition if suitable conditions are provided, e.g., in industrial composting or landfills 
[Lambert & Wagner, 2017]. Due to ambient temperature, the compostable bioplastics are not likely 
to degrade in open environments [Lambert & Wagner, 2017; Emadian et al., 2017]. Meanwhile, those 
bioplastics intended to biodegrade in the open environment, e.g., agricultural mulch films, are 
converted to carbon dioxide under ambient soil conditions [SAPEA, 2020]. However, a drawback 
could be that there is a potential that fragments of the mulch films may be transported by wind or 
rainfall into other open environmental compartments as the mulch films begin to biodegrade [SAPEA, 
2020]. 
Opacities of additives and chemical compositions in bioplastics 
Finally, the exact chemical compositions in bioplastics are unknown due to the opaque of what they 
contain [Bejgarn et al., 2015]. As mentioned in section 2.1, bioplastics do not exist without additional 
chemicals, as these are essential components of the bioplastic makeup [Lambert & Wagner, 2017]. 
Bioplastics face many challenges because, from a chemical perspective, the final products may be a 
cocktail of one or more polymers or blends with various additives, giving the product functionality 
[Lambert & Wagner, 2017; Zimmermann et al., 2019 and 2020a]. The polymers or the blends are not 
necessarily biodegradable [Lambert & Wagner, 2017]. Types of additives added into bioplastics can 
be mainly divided into four categories: functional additives (e.g., stabilizers, flame retardants, 
plasticizers, antioxidants, pro-oxidants, surfactants, and antistatic agents), colorants, fillers, and 
reinforcements, which all are used to improve the final products [Lambert & Wagner, 2017; 
Hahladakis et al., 2018; Gunaalan et al., 2020]. 
Furthermore, other chemicals can be present in bioplastic products, such as unreacted monomers and 
non-intentionally added substances (NIAS), which are impurities, by-products, or breakdown 
products [Wagner et al., 2024; Zimmermann et al., 2020a]. Bioplastic additives can vary widely, even 
for the same type of item or polymer, as different manufacturers will add different additives in various 
amounts [Lambert & Wagner, 2017; Gunaalan et al., 2020]. Thus, identifying the chemical 
composition of the bioplastics and potentially toxic compounds is complex, and the bioplastics made 
from the same polymer should be considered different chemically [Lambert & Wagner, 2017; 
Hahladakis et al., 2018]. Researchers conducting toxicity tests on bioplastics often might not know 
==PAGE:20==
 
20
the exact chemical compositions of the tested bioplastics, which can be attributed to the opaqueness 
of the information of additives presented in bioplastics from the producers [Afshar et al. 2024]. 
The chemical screenings of (bio)plastic leachates have more or less failed to identify chemical 
structures and composition, but slowly building knowledge about the content of bioplastic [Bejgarn 
et al., 2015; Zimmermann et al., 2019 and 2020a]. Zimmermann et al. (2020a) did non-targeted 
screenings to determine how many chemical features are in the extracts of various bioplastic products. 
They detected more than 1,000 chemical features in 80 % of 33 bioplastic products, most of them 
unique to one product, which illustrates the significant number and variety of low molecular weight 
chemicals present in bioplastic products. 67 % of the products contained toxic chemicals, and 
compared to conventional plastic products, bioplastic products are similarly toxic [Zimmermann et 
al., 2019 and 2020a]. This highlights that non-targeted screening is relevant for investigating and 
monitoring chemicals in bioplastic materials and products. 
Also, by conducting toxicity tests, Zimmermann et al. (2019 and 2020a) concluded that the raw 
materials applied to manufacture the bioplastic products were less toxic than the final products. This 
indicates that new chemicals were added or generated during bioplastics manufacturing, such as from 
side reactions or degradations of additives, but cannot be ascertained because product information is 
lacking. Therefore, it is n necessary to assess the chemicals added to the bioplastic products. 
2.2 Daphnia magna ecotoxicity tests 
Ecotoxicology is a field where toxicology, ecology, and chemistry disciplines are involved in 
assessing the toxic effects of substances on species in ecosystems [Traas & van Leeuwen, 2007]. This 
thesis uses Daphnia magna ecotoxicity tests to evaluate the harmful effects of bioplastic and plastic 
leachates on the freshwater zooplankton Daphnia magna. In a D. magna ecotoxicity test, a test 
substance is exposed to a controlled environment, where a D. magna culture is maintained, and factors 
such as water quality, temperature, and light are controlled [OECD, 2004]. The toxicity of leachates 
is assessed through the inhibition of mobilization and reproduction of D. magna. The results of these 
responses can be plotted as a relationship between the concentration of the substance and the effect, 
also known as a concentration-response curve. 
2.2.1 Concentration-response curve 
A concentration-response curve is an essential visual tool when working with ecotoxicity. With 
results from ecotoxicological tests, a sigmoidal curve can be set up, so a relationship between the 
concentration of a test substance and the response, in this case, immobilization of the zooplankton D. 
magna can be observed (Figure 2). 
The response of the test organism to the increasing concentration of the test substance is applied to 
determine the endpoint of relevance. For example, in this thesis, the endpoint of the effect 
concentration (ECX) is the estimated concentration of the test substance that will inhibit the 
mobilization of the test organism by percentages. Thus, an EC50 with, e.g., immobilization as an 
endpoint is where 50 % of the test organism population is immobilized in a predetermined period of 
time. 
==PAGE:21==
 
21
 
Figure 2: An example of a concentration-response curve. The NOEC, LOEC, EC10, and EC50 are illustrated in the graph. 
Two other commonly applied terms in toxicology are No Observed Effect Concentration (NOEC) 
and Lowest Observed Effect Concentration (LOEC). NOEC is the highest concentration of the test 
substance at which no effect is detected. The response is none and up to the threshold concentration; 
then, from this point, the response initiates and increases. Meanwhile, LOEC is the lowest 
concentration of the test substance at which an effect is detected. NOEC and LOEC can give an idea 
of the exposure limit of a test substance. 
2.2.2 Test organism Daphnia magna 
The zooplankton Daphnia magna is a key species in freshwater ecosystems [DTU, 2011]. D. magna 
is a filter-feeder and primarily eats algae and suspended particles (primary producers), and its entire 
lifespan takes approx. 60 days [DTU, 2011; Traas and van Leeuwen, 2007]. Also, the zooplankton is 
a prominent model organism in research and laboratory testing (Figure 3a).  
       
 
Figure 3a and b: a) Anatomy of D. magna [Traas and van Leeuwen, 2007]. b) asexual reproduction of D. magna [DTU, 2023]. 
The advantages of using D. magna in a laboratory are numerous because, for example, they [DTU, 
2011; Traas and van Leeuwen, 2007]: 
- 
are visible to the naked eye (0.5-5 mm) 
- 
are transparent: allowing observations of their inner at the microscope 
3a 
3b 
==PAGE:22==
 
22
- 
reproduces asexually (virgin birth): allows to generate clones (genetically identical) when the 
conditions are optimal (Figure 3b) 
- 
are easy to cultivate and maintain in the laboratory 
- 
have a relatively short life cycle (10 days) 
- 
filter a lot of water due to their feeding trait: one adult daphnia with a size of approx. 2-3 mm 
filtrates around 400 mL of water per day. This means that they have a huge contact with the 
surrounding water, and thus makes them quite sensitive to chemicals in the aqueous phase 
The clones of D. magna applied in this thesis ecotoxicity tests are from the same parent daphnia taken 
from Langedammen in Birkerød in 1978 and have continually maintained in the DTU laboratory 
[DTU, 2011]. D. magna is bred in glasses with 800 mL M7 media prepared from ultrapure Milli-Q 
water, salts, and vitamins (the content of the M7 media is detailed in Appendix E). Each 800 mL of 
M7 contains 12 parent D. magna. 
2.3 Ecotoxicity of complex leachates 
As bioplastics and plastics are not only composed of polymers, as described in section 2.1.1, 
bioplastic and plastic leachates may, therefore, consist of a cocktail of additives, polymer fragments, 
and metals. The definition of leachate is often referred to as the landfill by-product, where runoff 
water, e.g., rainwater, infiltrates through deposited waste, forming contaminated water that may 
contain heavy metals and organic and inorganic elements [Cambridge Dictionary, 2024]. This thesis 
defines leachate as a solution where a bioplastic or plastic product has the ultrapure water Milli-Q 
seeped through its matrix for 10 or 20 days. During this time, the product has leached out a mix of 
unknown, suspended elements from its matrix to a newly produced leachate. Consequently, it can be 
challenging to chart a leachate and to determine the components that might contribute to toxicity. 
Two approaches are used to assess the toxicity of a complex cocktail with unknown components and 
potentially pinpoint the toxicant: Whole Effluent Toxicity (WET) and Toxicity Identification 
Evaluation (TIE). 
The WET and TIE approaches are founded on the procedures the United States Environmental 
Protection Agency manufactured in 1991 and 1993. The WET and TIE approaches can be combined 
with the acute and chronic Daphnia magna ecotoxicity tests. 
2.3.1 Whole effluent toxicity 
Originally, the whole effluent toxicity assessment method is applied to monitor wastewater and 
prevent negative consequences of effluent on receipt streams and, thus, the ecosystem [Ra et al., 2015; 
US EPA, 1991]. The WET assessments are more explicitly applied to the effluent, which may contain 
unknown chemical and pollutant mixtures, to describe the toxicity effects on aquatic organisms [Ra 
et al., 2015; US EPA, 1991]. The ecotoxicity test of complex leachates is founded on the WET 
principles, where the toxic effects of leachates are tested on a specific aquatic organism. In this case, 
the freshwater species D. magna is applied. 
As effluent and bioplastic or plastic leachates are not the same, they are complexes of unknown 
chemical mixtures, and thus, the WET assessment approach can be transferred to leachates. In the 
==PAGE:23==
 
23
WET, the toxicity of the effluent is assessed, so a test organism is exposed to a range of concentrations 
of the effluent, e.g., 100 %, 50 %, 25 %, and so on of the concentration. The WET assessments are 
crucial in determining whether the wastewater treatment facilities are allowed to discharge the 
effluent without further treatment. 
The WET assessment can be utilized in acute and chronic toxicity tests. The acute toxicity tests are 
applied to determine the acute effect of the effluent on the test species. The tests are performed in a 
short timescale, where the test period is 48 hours if the D. magna species is used. Meanwhile, chronic 
toxicity explains the adverse effects of effluent exposure on the test organism. The test extends longer, 
with a test period of 21 days if the D. magna species is used. 
As the WET assessment only screens the effluent for toxicity as an entity, it does not consider exactly 
which component in the effluent results in toxicity. Accordingly, by combining the WET assessment 
with the toxicity identification evaluation, narrowing down which of the components in the effluent 
is toxic can be achieved. 
2.3.2 Toxicity identification evaluation 
The toxicants in the effluent are determined through the TIEs, which include physicochemical 
manipulations of effluent [Ra et al., 2015; US EPA, 1991 and 1993]. The purpose of the 
manipulations is to characterize the physical/chemical properties of the effluent toxicant to 
accompanying toxicity tests and narrow down which of the components in the effluent are harmful 
[US EPA, 1991]. Each identification evaluation is designed to alter or render biologically unavailable 
a group of toxicants such as oxidants, cationic metals, non-polar organics, or chelatable metals [US 
EPA, 1991]. By repeating the toxicity identification evaluations using samples of a particular effluent 
collected over time, these screening tests can provide information on whether the characteristics of 
the toxicants causing toxicity are consistent [US EPA, 1991]. 
In wastewater monitoring, the effluent is tested for priority pollutants, where each pollutant in the 
effluent sample is measured and compared to the literature concerning the toxicity of explicit 
toxicants (e.g., the toxicants could be metals and organic chemicals) [Ra et al., 2015]. In the United 
States, the Environmental Protection Agency has directed that, as of 2014, 129 pollutants needed to 
be tested under the “Clean Water Act” [US EPA, 2014]. For the EU, the “Water Framework 
Directive” has directed that 45 pollutants shall be monitored, following the agreed environmental 
quality standards [Directive 2008/105/EC]. 
The drawback is that it is impossible to determine which and how each component in the effluent or, 
in this case, the plastic and bioplastic leachates contributes to the toxicity. The effluent toxicity can 
result from a single or a combination of two or more components, known as mixture toxicity 
[Kortenkamp et al., 2009; US EPA, 1991]. The TIE also does not consider bioavailability, pH, ionic 
strength, hardness, and alkalinity, decreasing the chances of a successful TIE [US EPA, 1991]. 
Another drawback is that several thousand chemicals must be tested, much more than the priority 
pollutants the US and the EU have agreed upon [Kortenkamp et al., 2009; US EPA, 1991]. This 
implies that it is exceptionally challenging to pinpoint the toxicant that causes the toxicity of the 
effluent. 
==PAGE:24==
 
24
2.3.3 TIE approach in the acute toxicity tests 
The TIE approach in the thesis is inspired by the TIE method on the effluent, meaning that the 
pollutants in the plastic or bioplastic leachates are not measured and compared to the literature, but 
the physicochemical manipulation of leachates is applied. The toxicant in the bioplastic leachates 
may be pinpointed by combining the TIE approach with the ecotoxicity test with D. magna by 
applying the physicochemical manipulation with activated carbon, ethylenediaminetetraacetate 
(EDTA), and non-filtration. 
Activated carbon 
Activated carbon is a non-selective “chemical sponge” that absorbs and alters most organic chemicals 
[US EPA, 1991]. The activated carbon contains an internal pore structure, and due to the high surface 
area and size of pores, it is characterized by its remarkable effect on absorbing organic pollutants 
[Cotruvo et al., 2014]. However, activated carbon has difficulty adsorbing the lower molecular 
weight, water-soluble organic compounds [Cotruvo et al., 2014]. Thus, after removing the activated 
carbon that has shaken-stirred overnight in the leachates, the toxicity in the plastic or bioplastic 
leachates may be reduced [US EPA, 1991]. 
EDTA 
To determine if the toxicity of the leachates is caused by cationic metals(s), EDTA, a potent chelating 
agent, is applied to bind those metals into complexes [US EPA, 1991]. Due to the complexing 
strength, EDTA-metal complexes often can displace other soluble forms, and the success of EDTA 
in reducing metal toxicity is due to factors of the pH of the solution, the type and speciation of the 
metal, and the binding affinity of EDTA for the metal versus the affinity of the metal for the tissues 
of the organism, namely, whether the metals are bioavailable to D. magna [US EPA, 1991]. Among 
the cations that EDTA can chelate are aluminum, cadmium, cobalt, copper, iron, lead, manganese 
(2+), nickel, strontium, and zinc, and relatively weak chelates with arsenic, mercury, calcium, and 
magnesium, and cannot complex anionic forms of metals [US EPA, 1991]. Knowing which metals 
are bonded by EDTA can be beneficial in narrowing down the possible toxicants. 
However, the complex formation rate is specific for various metals, and some metals may not be 
rendered non-toxic due to a slow complexation rate when immediately used for the toxicity test [US 
EPA, 1991]. Also, the complexation of metals proceeds at a rate that may vary according to the sample 
matrix [US EPA, 1991]. The pH of the sample spiked with EDTA should be checked as it may lower 
the pH depending upon the buffering capacity and the amount of EDTA added [US EPA, 1991]. 
Some metals, such as zinc, nickel, and cadmium, exhibit greater toxicity at elevated pH, and copper 
and lead show elevated toxicity at pH 6.2 [US EPA, 1993]. 
Non-filtration/Filtration 
Non-filtration manipulation is applied to TIE to consider whether the viscous, impure leachates 
contain some toxicity that would otherwise have been filtered out. Microplastics and particles may 
possibly occur in the viscous plastic and bioplastic leachates, rendering the adsorbed toxicants less 
==PAGE:25==
 
25
biologically available to D. magna [US EPA, 1991]. However, D. magna may be exposed to these 
toxicants by ingesting the particles, and the degree to which any toxicants exist adsorbed or in 
leachates depends on particle surface charge (or lack thereof), surface area, toxicant polarity and 
charge, and solubility [US EPA, 1991]. Thus, filtering particles from the leachates may remove the 
cause or sink of toxicants. 
During the filtration of the leachates, the effects of pH can occur, and changes in pH can result in the 
formation of insoluble complexes of metals [US EPA, 1991]. Similarly, organic chemicals in ionic 
or polar forms can be transformed into non-ionic or non-polar forms and/or adsorb onto particles [US 
EPA, 1991]. 
2.4 Metals and organic chemicals in plastics and bioplastics 
A recent report on chemicals in plastics demonstrated that, on the global level, more than 16,000 
known chemical compounds (including polymers, additives, and NIAS) are present in plastic 
materials and products, both intentional and unintentional, and only 6 % of these compounds are 
regulated internationally [Wagner et al., 2024]. In principle, intentionally added substances (IAS) are 
known, meaning they can be assessed and regulated, whereas NIAS is unknown and, therefore, is 
complex to evaluate and regulate [Wagner et al., 2024]. However, the chemical identities and 
structures of 25 % of known chemical compounds are lacking, and half of the compounds’ 
functionalities and applications need to be clarified [Wagner et al., 2024]. Over 10,000 chemical 
compounds lack hazard information, while more than 4200 are recognized as persistent, 
bioaccumulative, mobile, and/or toxic [Wagner et al., 2024]. However, only 1,500 chemical 
compounds in plastic materials and products are known to leach into liquids, while at least 6,300 
potentially can [Wagner et al., 2024]. 
Most of the known chemical compounds in plastics are organic chemicals, which account for 75 % 
of the compounds, while 6 % are inorganic chemicals, and the remaining compounds are missing 
information [Wagner et al., 2024]. Among the organic chemicals, well known additives are 
phthalates, brominated flame retardants, and BPA, some of which are currently subjected to bans or 
restrictions under the EU’s REACH Regulation [Gunaalan et al., 2020]. Among the inorganic 
chemicals, sources of metals, such as zinc, cadmium, aluminum, tin, lead, nickel, copper, chromium, 
cobalt, and manganese, come from additives, which can be toxic in high concentrations [Gunaalan et 
al., 2020; Markowicz & Szymańska-Pulikowska, 2019].  
2.4.1 Plastic and bioplastic leachates 
Very little is known today about the effects of leaching additives from plastics and bioplastics into 
aquatic environments [Bejgarn et al., 2015; Zimmermann et al., 2020a; Gunaalan et al., 2020]. Most 
of these additives are not covalently bound to the polymer of plastics and bioplastics, whereas the 
reactive organic additives are polymerized in the polymer [Hahladakis et al., 2018; Zimmermann et 
al., 2019 and 2020a; Gunaalan et al., 2020]. Van der Waals forces hold many additives together with 
polymers, which are weak forces of temporary attraction between one electron-rich molecule and 
another electron-poor molecule [Zimmermann et al., 2020b; General Biology, 2024].  
==PAGE:26==
 
26
The extent of additive leaching is influenced by the properties of the additive and polymer, which is 
also influenced by the receiving environment conditions [Bejgarn et al., 2015; Gunaalan et al., 2020]. 
The enhanced biodegradability of the bioplastics may also accelerate the leaching of the organic 
chemicals and metals, and the fragmentation from macro- to microplastics [Bejgarn et al., 2015; 
Gunaalan et al., 2020]. Also, it is becoming apparent that varied environmental parameters such as 
temperature and pH can relevantly structure the intensity of leaching [Gunaalan et al., 2020]. 
Today, the additives are often tested individually, but a recent shift in plastic additive toxicology has 
occurred, so extract or leachate from a given plastic material is tested for adverse effects as a chemical 
mixture [Gunaalan et al., 2020]. Evidence indicates that testing a substance individually represents a 
minimal part of the additives leached by plastics and bioplastics, and assessing the whole mixture of 
leachates is needed to find if the toxicity effects come from the additives, degraded polymer, adsorbed 
POPs or metals, or a combination of all [Gunaalan et al., 2020]. 
Chemicals used daily are assessed and regulated through the European legislation on chemicals 
(REACH), but conversely, the environmental impact assessments of polymers are not conducted 
[Haider et al., 2019]. Also, more studies have been conducted in the field of human toxicology than 
in environmental toxicology [Haider et al., 2019]. Therefore, ecotoxicological data for plastic, 
especially bioplastic, is scarce [Haider et al., 2019]. 
2.4.2 Existing examples of ecotoxicity tests on bioplastic leachates 
Ecotoxicity testing the bioplastic and plastic leachates is not novel, but it is relatively new within the 
field of toxicology, especially for bioplastics [Haider et al., 2019]. The toxicology of the leachates 
was most addressed to food security in relation to the migration of substances to food [Barbale et al., 
2021]. In this section, existing examples of ecotoxicity tests on bioplastic leachates are presented 
(Table 1). The examples are found by researching on the search databases DTU Findit and Web of 
Sciences and by manual searching when reading found scientific literature. The search terms used 
can be found in section 2.5. 
Overall, studies addressing the effects of bioplastic leachates on freshwater species, i.e., D. magna, 
are limited compared to marine and soil compartments. Therefore, further efforts are necessary to 
clarify the extent of the influence of the additives leached from bioplastics and whether these 
conditions may produce adverse effects on freshwater species and ecosystems.

polyethylene terephthalate, PBAT: polybutylene adipate terephthalate, PHB (PHAs): polyhydroxy butyrate, which is a type of 
polyhydroxyalkanoates, and PLA: poly(lactic acid).

2.4.3 Charting the ecotoxicity of bioplastic leachates 
To simplify additives in plastics and bioplastics, the chemical compounds are separated into organic 
and inorganic chemicals, where the organic are measured in carbon and nitrogen content, and the 
inorganics are explained as metals. 
Organic chemicals in bioplastic leachates 
As the additives added in plastic and bioplastics are mostly unknown, it is hard to determine which 
organic chemicals are toxic and if there is mixture toxicity when leached out from them. This thesis 
measures the carbon and nitrogen content of the plastic and bioplastic leachates after 10 and 20 days 
of shake-stirring in water. Also, chemical features can be determined by a non-targeted analysis. The 
chemical features are both the organic and inorganic compounds, but studying the features can 
determine how much chemical compounds are leached from the plastics and bioplastics. More 
detailed descriptions of the carbon and nitrogen content and non-targeted analysis will follow in 
sections 3.6 and 3.7. 
Metals in bioplastic leachates 
A metal analysis will help determine which metals are present in plastic and bioplastic leachates, and 
their concentrations are also measured. By comparing the measured metal concentrations and the 
TIEs on leachates, it may be possible to determine which metals may contribute to the toxicity to 
impact D. magna. This thesis compares the found effect concentrations (EC/LC50) of metals on D. 
magna (Figure 4) with the metal analysis on leachates. A more detailed description of the metal 
analysis will follow in section 3.6. 
 
Figure 4: The effect concentrations (EC/LC50) for different metals on D. magna. The bars represent an interval in which the effect 
concentrations for one metal lie within, while the dots represent a single EC/LC50 value for one metal. All values can be found in 
Appendix H.

2.5 Literature research - searching during the project period 
Searching for literature and scientific literature is an integral part of conducting a thesis, in which 
they will structure a basic knowledge of the main subject: ecotoxicity of bioplastic leachates to 
Daphnia magna. The knowledge will help support and provide a perspective, which will help create 
a good, structured discussion about the current and acquired knowledge during the thesis period. It 
should be noted that the main purpose of the thesis is to perform laboratory experiments on the 
ecotoxicity of bioplastic leachates to D. magna. Therefore, systematic literature has not been 
conducted. However, literature needs to be researched to obtain knowledge beforehand to give 
perspective and overview of where the current knowledge about the ecotoxicity of bioplastic leachates 
stands. The search databases used to obtain literature are DTU Findit and Web of Science, and the 
search terms used can be reviewed in Table 2. Many search results were irrelevant because much of 
the scientific literature primarily focuses on the toxicity of plastic and bioplastic microplastics. Also, 
the term leachate mostly means landfill leachate.

3 Methods and Procedures 
Henceforth, the term (bio)plastic(s) will now refer to both bioplastic(s) and conventional plastic(s). 
In this section, the setups and methods of tests are explained. The thesis was constructed around the 
OECD guidelines no. 202 and no. 211, for the acute and chronic toxicity tests, respectively [OECD, 
2004; OECD, 2012]. Also, an approach founded on the whole effluent toxicity and toxicity 
identification evaluations was applied.  
Five overall experiments were performed. First, a reference test was conducted to ensure the test 
conditions were reliable and to evaluate and confirm the fitness of the species Daphnia magna culture. 
Second, three rounds of acute toxicity tests were conducted, two of which were conducted with water-
extracted (bio)plastic leachates. The (bio)plastics were shake-stirred in Milli-Q water for 10 and 20 
days, allowing the substances in the matrix of the (bio)plastics, such as metals and chemicals, to leach 
into the water. In the last acute toxicity test, the (bio)plastics were treated with methanol and dimethyl 
sulfoxide (DMSO). The methanol/DMSO method was an attempt to accelerate the leaching of metals 
and chemicals from the (bio)plastics.  
Finally, following the results of the acute toxicity tests, a chronic toxicity test, spanning over 21 days, 
was carried out with 20-day water-extracted (bio)plastic leachates, resembling the second acute test. 
Only two (bio)plastics were selected for the test: the shopping bag and the bio-waste bag. 
Bioplastic and plastic products used in tests and leachates thereof in this thesis are described. The 
data processing of the effect concentrations and concentration-response curves is also enlightened 
here. Analyses of metals, carbon, and nitrogen were conducted on (bio)plastic leachates, along with 
a non-targeted analysis, where analysis principles are explained. Statistical analyses are also 
described. 
3.1 (Bio)plastic products 
Table 3: (Bio)plastics applied in ecotoxicity tests. Information about the product name, corporation, polymer origin, and polymer
