In the ecotoxicity tests, three bioplastics and two conventional plastics were applied. The (bio)plastics 
used in this thesis are described in Table 3. Information found for (bio)plastics was obtained from 
various product manufacturer websites and the labels of the (bio)plastics. Information for the 
shopping bag was found from XingYuan Packaging (2024), while the food-waste bag was found from 
Abena (2024) and Mater-Bi (2024). Information for the bio-waste bag, the conventional waste bag, 
and the grocery carrier bag was found on their labels. 
3.2 Acute toxicity test - Acute immobilization test 
The acute toxicity tests were conducted in accordance with the OECD standard method no. 202 
[OECD, 2004]. In the acute toxicity test, the test organism zooplankton D. magna was exposed to 
(bio)plastic leachates at a range of test concentrations for 48 hours. The species D. magna was less 
than 24 hours old at the start of the test. The endpoint of the test was immobilization (unable to keep 
swimming within 15 seconds of observation), and the effect concentrations thereof at 24 and 48 hours 
were calculated. A more detailed description of the test can be reviewed in section 2.2. A reference 
test should be done before the acute toxicity test to ensure that the test conditions are reliable. 
The acute toxicity test applied a water-extraction approach to compare what would happen if 
(bio)plastics were wrongfully disposed of in the aquatic environment. The scenario was that when 
the (bio)plastics were in contact with an aquatic environment, they may slowly leach substances into 
the environment. Thus, in the laboratory, the leaching of (bio)plastic was done by shake-stirring them 
in ultrapure Milli-Q water for 10 or 20 days.  
3.2.1 Reference test 
A reference test should preferably be done before any ecotoxicity tests to ensure that the test 
conditions are reliable. Potassium dichromate (K2Cr2O7) is applied as a reference substance. A 24-
hour EC50 should be within the range of 0.6-2.1 mg/L. The reference test was carried out according 
to OECD guideline no. 202 and the DTU version for the acute toxicity test [DTU, 2011; OECD, 
2004]. 
A stock solution of 50 mg/L potassium dichromate to five test concentrations of 4.0, 2.0, 1.0, 0.5, and 
0.25 mg K2Cr2O7 per liter were used in the reference test. The effect concentration of the performed 
reference test was within the range with a concentration of 1.3 and 0.7 mg/L in 24-hour and 48-hour 
EC50. The result and concentration-response curves can be found in Appendix A.1. 
3.2.2 Production of (bio)plastic leachates 
The leachates of (bio)plastics were produced by letting them leach in Milli-Q water for ten or twenty 
days. Each of the five (bio)plastic products was cut into 1.5 x 1.5 centimeters pieces. Each half-liter 
flask contained 5 g of one (bio)plastic product. There were two replicates for each (bio)plastic 
product, so ten flasks in total were used. All flasks were filled up with 500 mL of Milli-Q water. One 
replicate of each (bio)plastic product was shake-stirring for ten days, while the other replicate was 
shake-stirring for twenty days on a platform shaker (INFORS-HT Orbitron) at 150 rpm.  
==PAGE:32==
 
32
   
 
Figure 5: Left) Water-extracted leachates from (bio)plastic products. All (bio)plastic products were shake-stirring for ten or twenty 
days in 500 mL Milli-Q water each. Approx. 20 mL of each (bio)plastic leachate was removed as unfiltered for application in a 
toxicity identification evaluation. Afterward, the leachates were filtered and transferred separately to new flasks as stock solutions. 
Right) Approx. 25 to 40 mL of filtered (bio)plastic leachates were taken from each stock solution for application in toxicity 
identification evaluations. The leachates were treated with either EDTA or activated carbon. 
Me+: metals, and benzene-ring: chemicals. 
Subsequently, the leachates of (bio)plastic were filtered with a large mesh screen (1 mm) to remove 
the larger pieces of (bio)plastic. Approx. 20 mL of leachate from every flask was removed as 
unfiltered for a toxicity identification evaluation (see sections 3.2.5 and 3.2.6). The remaining 
leachates were filtered in 0.45 and 0.22 µm Agilent Technologies’ Captiva Econo Filters and 
transferred into ten new 500 mL annealed flasks, five after ten days and five after twenty days, as 
stock solutions (Figure 5 left side). The leachates were filtered through many Agilent filters as the 
leachates were very viscous. There were no numbers on how many filters were used, so the leachates 
are listed in order from the most filters that have been used to the least on the leachates as: Shopping 
bag > Food-waste bag = Bio-waste bag > Grocery carrier bag = Conventional waste bag. 
3.2.3 Description of the immobilization test 
Test solutions 
The test solutions of (bio)plastic leachates were prepared by applying stock solutions described in 
section 3.2.2 and M7 media as dilution media. As the (bio)plastic leachates were water-extracted in 
ultrapure Milli-Q water, the stock solutions have no nutrition. Therefore, D. magna will not survive 
==PAGE:33==
 
33
without applying the M7 salts. Accordingly, the salts to make M7 media were added to the 100 % 
leachate stock solutions. The purpose was to keep the leachate concentration as close to 100 % as 
possible for the following tests. After adding M7 salts, the total percentage of leachate in each 
prepared test solution was 98 %. The content and volume of the different salts for the M7 media can 
be found in Appendix E. 
Apparatus 
All apparatus that would encounter the test solutions with leachate should be made entirely of glass 
or other materials chemically inert to the test chemical. The following equipment and materials were 
required for one test set (a set was with one stock solution of one (bio)plastic leachate) (variations in 
equipment use might occur): 
- M7 media as dilution media and M7 salts 
- Stock solutions of the (bio)plastic leachates 
- Acid and base for adjusting pH 
- Test beakers in glass (four for each concentration percentage and control) 
- Different-sized beakers for leachates and M7 solutions mixing 
- 
Plastic trays 
- Oxygen-meter and pH-meter for measuring oxygen (O2) and pH 
- Temperature-regulated room (20 ±2 °C) 
- 1- and 0.15-mm sized nylon mesh nets for capture and transfer of daphnids 
- Different-sized pipettes 
- Waterproof pen for labeling on glass 
- Petri dish and nylon mesh papers for capture and transfer of daphnids into beakers 
Test organism 
Freshwater species Daphnia magna 
Validity of test 
For the test to be valid, the criteria should be met: 
- 
O2 concentration must be ≥ 2 mg O2/L 
- 
Control immobilization must not exceed 10 % 
- 
The temperature should vary no more than ±2 °C during the entire test period 
- 
pH must not vary more than ±1.5 units from the initial pH 
- 
The 24-hour EC50 of the potassium dichromate (K2Cr2O7) from the reference test should be 
within the range of 0.6-2.1 mg/L 
3.2.4 Course of procedure 
A predetermined range of test concentrations and one control were applied. Five different test 
concentrations of a (bio)plastic leachate (100, 50, 25, 12.5, and 6.25 %) were applied in this case. 
Control was only M7 media with no leachate added. There were four replicates for each test 
concentration and control. 
==PAGE:34==
 
34
The test started by preparing the test solutions described in section 3.2.3 under “Test solutions”. The 
pH and O2 concentrations of the prepared mixed test solution for each (bio)plastic leachate and control 
must be measured before the distribution into beakers in predetermined test concentrations. If 
necessary, the pH of the test solution should be adjusted. The pH must be approx. the measured pH 
± 1.5 of the M7 media, and the O2 concentrations must be >2 mg/L. 
 
Figure 6: One test set of the acute toxicity test for one stock solution of a (bio)plastic leachate. The test concentration percentages of 
leachate were 100, 50, 25, 12.5, and 6.25 %. Each test concentration and control had four replicates. Each test beaker received five 
D. magna neonates. 
The prepared test solutions were distributed in test beakers, where the volume was equivalent to the 
concentration percentage. The total volume used for each (bio)plastic could vary as few of the 
(bio)plastic leachates were lost in filtering. At the same time, the (bio)plastic leachates were 
distributed between my acute toxicity tests and the tests my co-supervisor conducted. The total 
volumes were determined based on how much each (bio)plastic leachate was. All beakers were then 
diluted to the total volume with M7 media, except the 100 % concentration. All beakers were placed 
on a tray in rows, as shown in Figure 6. 
A healthy culture of D. magna was used as mother organisms, where they were more than 10, 
preferably 14, days old. The day before the test, the mother culture was cleaned up and ready for the 
next day. Therefore, the neonates of D. magna present the next day were born at night and thus were 
less than 24 hours old. On the day of the test, the neonates of D. magna were caught with two 
different-sized nylon mesh nets: one large mesh for parent D. magna and one small mesh for neonates. 
The neonates of D. magna from different cultures were pooled before being added to test beakers. 
For each beaker, five neonates were added with nylon mesh papers. The total number of neonates in 
each beaker was controlled one more time. They were not fed during the test. All beakers were placed 
in darkness in a temperature-regulated room. 
==PAGE:35==
 
35
At 24 and 48 hours after the start of the test, the number of immobilized D. magna was counted. If a 
D. magna did not move when a needle was held close to it, it was considered immobilized. Results 
were noted, and the effect concentrations were later calculated. The pH and O2 concentrations were 
measured again at the end of the test in one replicate of the control and the highest test concentration. 
3.2.5 Toxicity identification evaluations in acute immobilization test 
Toxicity identification evaluations (TIEs) were accomplished with the acute immobilization test. The 
leachates were evaluated through physicochemical manipulations, and the explicit pollutant that 
might contribute to the toxicity was targeted. The targeted toxicants in the TIEs were metals, 
chemicals, and unfiltered leachates. The leachates applied must be treated differently for each TIE, 
and further steps were needed. A more detailed description of the test can be reviewed in section 
2.3.3. 
3.2.6 Production of (bio)plastics leachates to TIEs 
Note that the M7 salts were added in all TIE test solutions as done in section 3.2.3 under “Test 
solutions”. Thus, the content of nutrients was the same as the test solutions in the ordinary acute 
toxicity test to ensure consistent and accurate results. For each TIE, the M7 salts were added in 
different ways (Figure 5 right side). In the production of leachates with EDTA or unfiltered leachates, 
the salts were added directly to the 100 % leachate stock solutions. In the production of leachates 
with activated carbon, the salts were provided after the treatment and filtration of the leachates. 
Activated carbon to neutralize chemicals 
Unfiltered leachate was used to produce a test solution with activated carbon. The activated carbon 
was added to a flask with 20 mL leachate stock solution. The flask was shaken on a shaker platform 
(150 rpm) for 24 hours. Subsequently, the leachate was filtered through a 0.45 and/or 0.22 µm filter, 
as done in section 3.2.2. 
EDTA to neutralize metals 
To neutralize metals, EDTA was added to a test solution. A stock solution of EDTA was produced 
with a concentration of 600 mg EDTA per liter of Milli-Q. For a test solution, the volume of the 
added EDTA stock solution should be calculated at 1 mg/L as the final concentration of EDTA. 
Unfiltered (bio)plastic leachates 
The unfiltered leachates were obtained from each stock solution before filtering, as described in 
section 3.2.2. 
Distribution and dilutions of the test solutions 
In the final step, all prepared test solutions were distributed into test beakers, where the volume was 
equivalent to the concentration percentage of 25 %. The M7 media were added to the predetermined 
==PAGE:36==
 
36
total volume. Again, the total volume could vary, as a few of the (bio)plastic leachates were lost in 
filtering. Furthermore, the leachates were distributed between my co-supervisor and me. 
3.3 Acute immobilization test with methanol and dimethyl sulfoxide 
In the preliminary and second acute toxicity tests with water-extracted (bio)plastic leachates, the 
leachates did have few to no effects on D. magna. Inspired by three articles written by Zimmermann 
et al. (2019, 2020a, and 2020b), who investigated the toxicities of (bio)plastics by using in vitro 
bioassays (2019 and 2020a) and D. magna (2020b), the production of the (bio)plastic leachates was 
changed. 
Zimmermann et al. 2019 and 2020a applied methanol as a solvent to extract metals and chemicals 
from (bio)plastic products. Methanol was chosen as a solvent because it did not dissolve the 
(bio)plastic polymers but only released the substances in the matrix of (bio)plastics. After that, they 
used dimethyl sulfoxide (DMSO) as a keeper for the leached chemicals and metals [Sigma-Aldrich, 
2024]. A keeper is a substance with reduced volatility that adsorbs the target analyte (metals and 
chemicals) and is kept during an evaporative procedure, such as under a stream of nitrogen 
[Dąbrowski, 2016; Zimmermann et al., 2019]. The method was used to try to extract more metals and 
chemicals from the (bio)plastic products. 
Zimmermann’s plastic equivalent 
Zimmermann et al. 2019 and 2020a established a “plastic equivalent” method, which was used to 
contextualize the bioassay results, where “1 mg plastic” equals the toxicity extracted from 1 mg of 
plastic. Accordingly, Zimmermann et al. used 200 µL DMSO on methanol-dissolved 3000 mg of 
plastic, corresponding to 15 mg of plastic per 1 µL DMSO extract. 
(Bio)plastic equivalent 
Suppose Zimmermann’s plastic equivalent was used similarly for the previous acute toxicity tests. In 
that case, the (bio)plastic equivalent corresponded to the (bio)plastic-water ratio. In both tests, 5 g of 
one (bio)plastic product was shake-stirred in a flask with half-liter Milli-Q water. Thus, the 
(bio)plastic equivalent ((bio)pl.-eq.) was: 
(Bio)plastic −equivalent = 5000 mg (bio)plastic
500 mL Milli −Q water = 10
mg (bio)plastic
mL Milli −Q water 
3.3.1 Translating the (bio)plastic leachate percentages with the (bio)plastic equivalent 
The test concentrations for the new acute toxicity test must be selected. This was done by translating 
the (bio)plastic leachate percentages used in previous acute toxicity tests with the (bio)plastic 
equivalent to know how much mg of (bio)plastic test concentrations contained. The EC50_Repro for 
PLA bioplastic from Zimmermann et al., 2020b, was also used to select appropriate test 
concentrations. 
==PAGE:37==
 
37
If using a total volume of 20 mL of a (bio)plastic leachate from the previous acute tests, the 
concentration percentages of (bio)plastic, translated with the (bio)plastic equivalent, were to what is 
shown in Figure 7. Thus, the previous tests with concentration percentages of 100, 50, 25, 12.5, and 
6.25 % contained 10, 5, 2.5, 1.25, and 0.625 mg (bio)pl./mL, respectively. 
 
Figure 7: The (bio)plastic leachate concentration percentages translated with the (bio)plastic equivalent into milligrams of 
(bio)plastic per mL Milli-Q water. 
The shopping bag and the bio-waste bag leachates were the only (bio)plastic that could produce the 
effect concentrations in the second acute toxicity test. The total volumes of 8 and 10 mL were used 
for the shopping bag and the bio-waste bag leachates in the second test (Table 29 in App. B.1). 
Suppose the EC50 of both leachates were applied with the (bio)plastic equivalent, the concentrations 
of (bio)plastics that immobilize 50 % of D. magna could be found. The EC50 of the shopping bag at 
48 hours was 76 %, while the bio-waste bag had EC50 of 68 % (Table 36 in Appendix B.1). These 
concentration percentages corresponded to 7.6 and 6.8 mg (bio)pl./mL for the shopping bag and the 
bio-waste bag, respectively. 
Comparing the EC50_Repro for PLA bioplastic from Zimmermann et al., 2020b, which was 122 mg/L 
(0.122 mg/mL), the calculated (bio)plastic equivalents for the shopping bag and the bio-waste bag 
were far higher than the (bio)plastic equivalent of PLA. It should be noted that the EC50 was from a 
chronic toxicity test. 
Based on the calculations, seven test concentrations were selected for the next acute toxicity test: 10, 
5, 2.5, 1.25, 0.625, 0.313, and 0.156 mg (bio)pl./mL, with five replicates. Two EDTA-TIEs of 10 and 
2.5 mg (bio)pl./mL were also applied to the next test, where the 10 mg (bio)pl./mL only had three 
replicates. Also, a total volume of 20 mL was chosen for all test concentrations. 
3.3.2 DMSO concentrations in test solutions and potential toxicity 
If Zimmermann’s plastic equivalent of 15 mg plastic per 1 µL extract was selected, the total volume 
of DMSO for 10 mg (bio)pl./mL in a total test volume of 20 mL was calculated as: 
Volume of DMSO =
10 (bio)pl./mL
15 mg (bio)plastic
μL DMSO
· 20 mL = 13.33 μL DMSO 
The total needed volume of DMSO for all test concentrations and replicates combined was approx. 
189 µL. How much of each (bio)plastic leachate extract was required can be calculated as:  
==PAGE:38==
 
38
(Bio)plastic leachate extract = plastic equivalent · volume of DMSO
= 15 mg (bio)plastic
μL DMSO
· 189 µL DMSO = approx. 2,835 mg 
To ensure enough leachate extracts for the acute test, 3 grams of (bio)plastic for each (bio)plastic 
product was chosen to extract, which corresponded to a volume of 200 µL DMSO. 
Potential toxicity of DMSO 
For the highest concentration of (bio)plastic, the volume of DMSO would be under 1 %, as 
Zimmermann et al., 2019 and 2020a also did. This ensures that DMSO was not the toxic component 
in the toxicity test. When diluting with M7 media to the total volume of 20 mL, the 10 mg (bio)pl./mL 
test solution would contain a maximum of 0.07 % DMSO because the equation showed that: 
Percentage of DMSO =
0.013 mL DMSO
Total volume of 20 mL · 100 % = 0.065 % 
The total volume of 20 mL contained DMSO, which was far under 1 %, and still contained 10 mg 
(bio)pl./mL. 
3.3.3 Production of (bio)plastic leachates with methanol and DMSO 
Based on the results from the preliminary and second acute toxicity tests, only the shopping bag, the 
bio-waste bag, and the conventional waste bag were applied in the new acute toxicity test. The 
shopping and bio-waste bags were selected because of their possible induced toxicities on D. magna. 
The conventional waste bag was chosen because it can be compared to the bio-waste bag as they have 
the same polymer type (chemically identical). 
All three (bio)plastics were cut into 1.5 x 1.5 centimeters pieces. 3 grams of each (bio)plastic product 
was added into amber glass vials. In each glass, 20 mL methanol (99.9 %, Sigma-Aldrich) was added, 
and then they were extracted by sonication in an ultrasound bath for one hour at room temperature. 
Subsequently, the methanol with leachates of (bio)plastics was filtered with a large mesh screen to 
remove the larger pieces of (bio)plastic and transferred into new clean glasses. 200 μL of each 
leachate was retained for chemical analysis. 
200 μL of DMSO (99.99 %, Fluka) was added into the new glasses with methanol and leachates. 
DMSO was used as a keeper, which adsorbed the chemicals and metals extracted from (bio)plastics. 
DMSO was also chosen because it does not evaporate under a stream of nitrogen, as the methanol in 
glasses was evaporated with nitrogen to the final volume of 200 μL. The final extract solutions were 
then stored in the 4 C room. 
3.3.4 Description and course of the procedure of the test with methanol and DMSO 
The extract stock solution must be diluted before distribution to test beakers. Each 200 μL DMSO 
extract was diluted with 300 mL M7 media, and a (bio)plastic equivalent of 10 mg (bio)plastic/mL 
M7 media was obtained. The highest test concentration received 20 mL for each replicate, 
corresponding to 10 mg (bio)pl./mL, while the second-highest test concentration received half as 
much and continued with the remaining test concentrations. The M7 media were added in test beakers, 
==PAGE:39==
 
39
except for the test concentration of 10 mg (bio)pl./mL, to obtain a final total volume of 20 mL. The 
ratio between the (bio)plastic extract and M7 media is shown in Table 4.  
To ensure that all the 200 μL extract came out of the glass vial, approx. 5 mL M7 media was added 
and shaken in the vial with the lid closed. The shaken media with the remaining extract was poured 
out from the vial, and the procedure was repeated until the bottom and wall of the glass vial were 
“clean”. 
All the apparatus, the test organism, and the criteria for validity of the test were the same as in the 
previous acute toxicity tests in section 3.2.3, with a few additions. M7 salts were not added to the test 
solutions as they were diluted with M7 media. Instead of using different-sized beakers, a 300 mL 
measuring flask for a test solution to mix with the M7 media was used.

The course of procedure for the acute toxicity test with methanol and DMSO treatment was exact as 
the previous acute toxicity tests in section 3.2.4. The only change was that a solvent control was 
applied, which was Milli-Q water with methanol and DMSO treatment without any (bio)plastic, 
which was treated identically to the process of producing (bio)plastic extracts (section 3.3.3). This 
ensured that the DMSO solvent was not the toxic component in the toxicity test. 
3.4 Chronic toxicity test - Reproduction test 
The chronic toxicity test was conducted in accordance with the OECD standard method no. 211 
[OECD, 2012]. The reproduction test was a semi-static chronic toxicity test in which the test organism 
zooplankton D. magna was exposed to (bio)plastic leachates at a range of test concentrations for 21 
days. The endpoint of the test was reproduction, where the total number of living offspring produced 
was assessed. The time to produce the first brood and the living offspring produced per D. magna per 
day was also counted and noted. The mobility and mortality of parent D. magna were also reported. 
The reproductive outputs were interpreted in the relationship between the concentration of the 
(bio)plastic leachates and the effect thereof. The acute toxicity tests were done before the reproduction 
test, which helped select an appropriate test concentration range. 
As in the first two acute toxicity tests in section 3.2, the water-extraction approach was chosen in this 
chronic toxicity test. The approach was selected based on results from both types of acute toxicity 
tests, where the (bio)plastics shake-stirred in ultrapure Milli-Q water for 20 days did impact D. magna 
to a greater extent compared to those in 10 days and to methanol/DMSO treatment. 
==PAGE:40==
 
40
3.4.1 Production of bioplastic leachates 
Only bioplastics, the shopping and bio-waste bags, were applied in the reproduction test. The two 
bioplastics were selected based on the results from the preliminary, second, and third acute toxicity 
tests. Also, only two bioplastics were chosen because a reproduction test was an extensive test that 
ran over a long period. One reproduction test with an appropriate test concentration range required 
many glasses. Hence, many D. magna neonates and a decent amount of bioplastic leachate, which 
takes 20 days to produce, were required. The production of leachates of the bioplastics was prepared 
in the same way as in section 3.2.1. Four replicates for one bioplastic were applied, so with two 
bioplastics, eight flasks were used. The leachates were not filtered. 
3.4.2 Description of the reproduction test 
A predetermined range of test concentrations and one control was applied, where three different test 
concentrations of one bioplastic leachate were used (25, 12.5, and 6.25 %). Control was only M7 
media with no leachate added. There were ten replicates for each test concentration percentage and 
control. The stock solutions were diluted with M7 media to reach the predetermined test concentration 
percentages of the leachate. 
Apparatus 
All apparatus that would encounter the test solutions with leachate should be made entirely of glass 
or other materials chemically inert to the test chemical. The following equipment and materials were 
required for one test set (a set was with one stock solution of one (bio)plastic leachate) (variations in 
equipment use might occur): 
- 
M7 media 
- 
Stock solutions of the bioplastic leachates 
- 
Ten glass test beakers per test concentration percentage and control 
- 
Plastic trays 
- 
Two 500 mL and three 50 mL volumetric flasks 
- 
Three large beakers 
- 
Oxygen-meter and pH-meter for measuring O2 and pH 
- 
Apparatus for the control of the lighting regime 
- 
Temperature-regulated room (20 ±2 °C) 
- 
1- and 0.15-mm sized nylon meshes for capture and transfer of daphnids 
- 
Small nylon fishing net for capture and transfer of parent daphnids 
- 
Different-sized pipettes 
- 
Waterproof pen for labeling on glass 
- 
Petri dish and nylon mesh papers for capture, counting, and transfer of neonates 
- 
Living algal Pseudokirchneriella subcapitata 
- 
Centrifuge Henttich Zentrifugen Rontanta 460R (centrifugation of the algae) 
- 
Multisizer Coulter Counter (counting algae cells) 
==PAGE:41==
 
41
Test organism 
Freshwater species Daphnia magna 
Validity of test 
For the test to be valid, the following criteria should be met in control(s): 
- 
The mortality of the parent D. magna does not exceed 20 % at the end of the test 
- 
The mean number of living offspring produced per parent D. magna surviving at the end of 
the test is  60 
For all test sets and control(s): 
- 
The temperature should be within the range of 18-22 °C and should vary no more than ±2 °C 
within these limits as a daily range during the entire test period 
- 
16 hours of light at an intensity not exceeding 15-20 μE·m-2·s-1 is needed 
- 
pH must not vary more than ±1.5 units from the pH of the M7 media (preferably an adjusted 
pH of 8.20±0.03 before the start of the test) 
- 
O2 concentration must have been at least ≥3 mg O2/L at the beginning, during, and end of the 
test 
3.4.2 Course of procedure 
The test started by preparing the test solutions. The pH and O2 concentrations of the control and all 
concentration percentages of each bioplastic must be measured before the distribution into beakers in 
predetermined test concentrations. If necessary, the pH of the test solution should be adjusted with 
acid and base. The pH must be around the pH of the M7 media (approx. pH 8.20), and the O2 
concentrations must be >3 mg/L. A total volume of 50 mL in each test beaker was selected.  
The procedure for processing D. magna was the same as in section 3.2.4, but only one neonate D. 
magna was transferred to each test beaker in this test. The test solutions and control media were 
changed every second or third day. The next series of test beakers with test solutions and control was 
prepared before the parent D. magna was transferred with a small nylon fishing net. The pH and O2 
concentrations of the control, old, and new test series were measured on the changing day. The old 
test solutions transferred with the D. magna should be minimized. 
During the test, D. magna was fed a diet of living algal cells of Pseudokirchneriella subcapitata every 
second or third day. The diet was given in rations according to the OECD standard no. 211, based on 
the amount of organic carbon (C) provided to each parent D. magna. The ratio level was between 0.1 
and 0.2 mg C/Daphnia/day, and the organic carbon was measured as cell number with the Multisizer 
Coulter Counter. A lower ratio was provided initially and increased with the growth of the D. magna. 
The diet of the algae was concentrated, which was obtained through centrifugation. Consequently, 
the volume of the algal culture medium transferred to the test beakers was minimized. 
When the first brood appeared, the time to produce these was noted. The living offspring produced 
per D. magna per day were counted and removed daily to prevent them from consuming food intended 
==PAGE:42==
 
42
for the parent. Additionally, the presence of aborted eggs or dead offspring was also recorded daily, 
as well as the mobility and mortality of D. magna. 
3.5 Calculations for the effect concentrations and concentration-response 
curves 
The effect concentrations can be calculated in an Excel spreadsheet once the acute toxicity test results 
are available. Thereafter, the concentration-response curves are set up in R.studio. The program 
R.studio applies regression methods to fit a concentration-response curve based on the calculated 
response and the range of concentrations of the test substance. 
In a spreadsheet, the responses, e.g., immobilization of D. magna, are calculated against the number 
of D. magna applied in the determined test concentration percentage of (bio)plastic leachate. The 
equation used to calculate the relationship between the response and the number of D. magna is shown 
below: 
Ratio =
Response (No. of immobilized 𝐷𝑎𝑝ℎ𝑛𝑖𝑎 𝑚𝑎𝑔𝑛𝑎)
Total No. of Daphnia magna in a concentration percentage of leachate · 100 % 
With the calculated values by applying the equation, a concentration-response curve can be set up in 
R.studio using the relationship between the response ratio and the concentration percentage of a 
(bio)plastic leachate in the log function. Values for the effect concentrations with 20 and 50 % 
responses are also found in R.studio. The R.studio version applied is 2022.12.0+353. The codes used 
in R.studio can be found in Appendix I. 
3.6 Analyses of (bio)plastic leachates and extracts 
(Bio)plastic leachates were analyzed for metals, carbon, and nitrogen, as well as a non-targeted 
analysis was conducted, while (bio)plastic extracts were analyzed for metals only. The analysis 
principles are explained in this section.  
3.6.1 Metal analysis 
To measure the metal concentrations in the (bio)plastic leachates and extracts, inductively coupled 
plasma-mass spectrometry (ICP-MS) and -optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES) are applied. 
ICP-MS is an elemental analysis technique that measures elements, rather than molecules and 
compounds, to trace levels in a sample (low detection limits) [Agilent, 2024a]. The machine converts 
the sample into ions with the help of argon plasma, and then the mass spectrometer measures the 
ionized sample [Agilent, 2024a]. 
ICP-OES is also an elemental analysis technique, but it measures elements that release light at specific 
wavelengths as they transition to a lower energy level [Agilent, 2024b]. This is done by exciting 
atoms and ions by the heat from an argon plasma [Agilent, 2024]. 
==PAGE:43==
 
43
3.6.2 Carbon and nitrogen analysis 
The carbon content was measured using a high-temperature combustion furnace and an acid reactor 
[Skalar, 2024]. Total organic carbon (TOC) is calculated by analyzing the total carbon (TC) and the 
inorganic carbon (IC) [Skalar, 2024]. The TC in a sample is converted to carbon dioxide (CO2) in a 
high-temperature combustion furnace at temperatures up to 950 °C by catalytic oxidation [Skalar, 
2024]. Subsequently, the CO2 dispersed into the carrier gas and was measured by a non-dispersive 
infrared detector (NDIR) [Skalar, 2024]. 
The IC is determined by injecting the sample into an acid reactor that converts the IC to CO2. The 
NDIR measures the concentration of CO2 formed during acidification [Skalar, 2024]. Furthermore, 
the TOC concentration is found by subtracting the TIC from the TC [Skalar, 2024]. 
Total nitrogen (TN) is combusted in the combustion furnace and analyzed as converted nitric oxide 
(NO) [Skalar, 2024]. Using chemiluminescence detection (CLD), where NO reacts to form nitrogen 
dioxide (NO2), the photons emitted from the rapid decay of the NO2 are detected by a photomultiplier 
tube [Skalar, 2024]. 
Since the (bio)plastic extracts contain methanol, which is combustible, the samples cannot be tested 
for carbon or nitrogen concentrations. 
3.6.3 Non-targeted analysis 
The non-targeted analysis was conducted using liquid chromatography-mass spectrometry (LC-MS). 
Liquid chromatography (LC) is a separation technique that separates the components of a sample at 
high pressure based on the differences in their affinity or retention strength for the stationary and 
mobile phases [Shimadzu, 2024; Eurofins, 2024]. After separating the components, the effluent is 
directed to the mass spectrometer [Eurofins, 2024]. 
Mass spectrometry (MS) is a technique that ionizes the effluent coming from LC by applying 
electromagnetic fields, separates the resulting ions in a vacuum based on their mass-to-charge ratios 
(m/z), and measures the intensity of each ion [Shimadzu, 2024; Eurofins, 2024]. Subsequently, a mass 
spectrum plots the relative ion intensities against the m/z values, generating a series of mass spectra 
at each time point [Shimadzu, 2024].  
With the coupling of LC with MS, the mass spectra obtained from these measurements provide 
molecular mass and structural information for eluted components, which supplement the qualitative 
analysis and data based on retention times [Shimadzu, 2024]. 
The MS spectrometry method has two polarity modes: negative and positive ion modes, each 
requiring precise mass spectrometry tuning. The difference is that the negative ion mode charges the 
analyte through deprotonation, while the positive ion mode charges through protonation [NorthEast 
Biolab, 2024]. 
==PAGE:44==
 
44
3.7 Statistical analysis 
This thesis applies statistical analysis, one-way ANalysis Of VAriance (ANOVA), and subsequent 
post hoc tests. One-way ANOVA compares the means across multiple groups and determines whether 
there are significant differences among them [DTU Compute, 2020]. ANOVA provides a 
comprehensive framework for exploring the variation within and between groups, enabling statistical 
evidence to reject or accept the null hypothesis, which expresses that the multiple groups have the 
same means [DTU Compute, 2020]. ANOVA uses F-statistic, which means if the between-group 
variability is significantly larger than the within-group variability (the F-value is large), there is 
evidence to support the presence of a significant difference between the groups, and the null 
hypothesis is rejected [DTU Compute, 2020]. Additionally, as done in this thesis, the F-value can be 
compared with a critical value derived from probability distributions (95 % confidence) to determine 
whether the observed differences are statistically significant [DTU Compute, 2020]. 
After conducting an ANOVA and establishing that there are significant differences between group 
means, post hoc pairwise tests are utilized to make pairwise comparisons between two selected groups 
[DTU Compute, 2020]. The pairwise tests can identify specific groups that differ significantly from 
each other, and they are identified by finding t-values and comparing them with the critical t-values 
with 95 % confidence [DTU Compute, 2020]. If a t-value is smaller than the critical t-value, the null 
hypothesis, which expresses that the two groups have the same mean, is accepted [DTU Compute, 
2020]. Otherwise, it is rejected. 
p-values are also found for both ANOVA and post hoc tests, where they are compared to a 
significance level of 0.05 [DTU Compute, 2020]. The null hypothesis is accepted if a p-value is larger 
than the significance level of 0.05 [DTU Compute, 2020]. Otherwise, it is rejected. 
To utilize the statistical analysis, the program R.studio is applied to calculate ANOVA and the 
subsequent post hoc tests. The R.studio version applied is 2022.12.0+353. The codes used in R.studio 
can be found in Appendix K. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:45==
 
45
4 Results 
All data from the experiments performed were collected over the period from 10th January to 17th 
May. Three acute toxicity tests and one chronic toxicity test were performed in that period. All raw 
data can be found in Appendices A to D. Metal, total carbon, total nitrogen, and non-targeted analyses 
were conducted, as well as finding microplastics under a microscope. The data can be found in 
Appendices F and G. 
4.1 Preliminary acute toxicity test and TIEs 
The first acute toxicity test explores whether some of the (bio)plastic releases toxic substances into 
the water. If this is the case, then the eventual next step is to perform chronic toxicity tests. The 10-
day-shaken leachates derived from five (bio)plastics are used, as described in sections 3.1 and 3.2. 
The acute toxicity test is based on the OECD guideline no. 202. The TIEs are based on Ra et al. 
(2015) and US EPA (1991 and 1993), which are used to chart whether organic chemicals or metals 
are the toxic components in the leachates. 
The pH and O2 concentrations for controls, all 100 % leachate test solutions, and all TIEs were stable 
from the start to the end. The values do not deviate from the criteria described in section 3.2.3 under 
“Validity of test”. 
Due to few D. magna offspring per day, the acute toxicity test with 10-day-shaken (bio)plastic 
leachates and the acute toxicity test with TIE-treated leachates were performed together over five 
parts over three weeks. There is always a control set (four replicates) for a (bio)plastic leachate set or 
a TIE. On some days, two sets or TIEs can be made in one day, so one control set can be used for one 
or more sets or TIEs. The date for each set, TIE, and control are in Appendix A.2 and A.3. 
4.1.1 Acute toxicity test with 10-day-shaken (bio)plastic leachates 
The first acute toxicity test was conducted from January 16th to January 26th, 2024. The stock solutions 
of (bio)plastic leachates are made as described in section 3.2.2. The method for the acute toxicity test 
is described in section 3.2. The raw data for the acute toxicity test is in Appendix A.2.  
Across all (bio)plastic leachates, very few D. magna are immobilized in the toxicity test. Apart from 
the 6.25 % shopping bag leachate, which has the most significant amount of immobilized D. magna 
for one replicate (20 % immobilized D. magna at 24 hours), the leachates that have an impact on D. 
magna are bio-waste bag and shopping bag. The 100 % bio-waste bag leachate has 14.3 % 
immobilized D. magna at 48 hours. Meanwhile, the result of immobilized D. magna in the shopping 
bag leachate shows a reverse concentration-response trend. Across the concentration percentages of 
the leachate, 100, 25, and 6.25 % leachate have 5, 10, and 20 % immobilized D. magna after 48 hours, 
respectively. It should be the converse since the highest concentration percentage should have 
impacted D. magna more than the lowest. Due to the lack of immobilized D. magna across all test 
concentrations, the data is incoherent; no effect concentration values can be calculated, and hence, no 
concentration-response curves are made. 
==PAGE:46==
 
46
4.1.2 Acute toxicity test with 10-day-shaken TIE-treated (bio)plastic leachates 
The first acute toxicity test with TIE-treated leachates was conducted from January 16th to February 
1st, 2024. The stock solutions of (bio)plastic leachates are made as described in sections 3.2.2 and 
3.2.6. The method for the acute toxicity test is described in section 3.2 and supplemented in sections 
3.3.5-3.3.6. The raw data for the acute toxicity test is in Appendix A.3. 
In this acute toxicity test, three TIEs are made for each (bio)plastic leachate. The applied 
physicochemical manipulations on leachates are activated carbon, EDTA, and non-filtration. Each 
TIE-treated leachate is diluted with M7 media to a leachate concentration of 25 % and distributed 
into four replicates. 
The EDTA-treated shopping bag and bio-waste bag leachates immobilize 100 % of D. magna at 24 
hours (Figure 8). The other EDTA-treated leachates impact 5 % to no D. magna. The activated 
carbon-treated shopping bag and conventional waste bag immobilize 5 and 25 % of D. magna at 48 
hours, respectively. Meanwhile, only the unfiltered grocery carrier bag impacts D. magna with 25 % 
immobilization at 24 hours. 
 
Figure 8: Responses in % for three TIEs on (bio)plastic leachates at 24 and 48 hours. 
4.2 Second acute toxicity test and TIEs 
The second acute toxicity test with 20-day-shaken (bio)plastic leachates is applied to explore if the 
longer exposure of the (bio)plastics releases more toxic substances into the water. The acute toxicity 
test is analogous to the preliminary acute toxicity test described in section 3.2. 
The pH and O2 concentrations for controls, all 100 % leachate test solutions, and all TIEs were stable 
from the start to the end, apart from the shopping bag leachate. The values did not deviate from the 
criteria described in section 3.2.3 under “Validity of test”. 
Due to few D. magna offspring per day, the acute toxicity test with 20-day-shaken (bio)plastic 
leachates and the acute toxicity test with TIE-treated leachates were performed together over four 
parts over three weeks. The procedure with control is the same as the preliminary test. The date for 
each set and control can be found in Appendix B.1 and B.2.

4.2.1 Acute toxicity test with 20-day-shaken (bio)plastic leachates 
The second acute toxicity test was conducted from February 6th to February 22nd, 2024. The stock 
solutions of (bio)plastic leachates are made as described in section 3.2.2. The method for the acute 
toxicity test is described in section 3.2. The raw data for the acute toxicity test is in Appendix B.1. 
The arrangement of each set for (bio)plastic leachates is the same as the preliminary test (section 
4.1.1), where the same test concentrations are applied with four replicates each. 
A problem occurred with the pH of the shopping bag leachate, which had the lowest pH of 7.4 and 
dropped to 6.9 at the end of the test. All D. magna were immobilized as a result of this. Consequently, 
the bio-waste bag leachate with a low pH of 7.7 is adjusted with acid. The pH is adjusted to 8.2, 
ensuring the pH did not drop as it happened to the shopping bag, but only to 7.8 after 48 hours. 
Due to the bad outcome of the toxicity test, a new stock solution for the shopping bag is made and is 
shaken in 20 days. The new test is conducted and can be found in Appendix B.3.

4.2.2 Acute toxicity test with 20-day-shaken TIE-treated (bio)plastic leachates 
The second acute toxicity test with TIE-treated leachates was conducted from February 6th to 
February 22nd, 2024. The stock solutions of (bio)plastic leachates are made as described in sections 
3.2.2 and 3.3.6. The method for the acute toxicity test is described in section 3.2 and supplemented 
in sections 3.3.5-3.3.6. The raw data for the acute toxicity test is found in Appendix B.2. 
Four TIEs are made for each (bio)plastic leachate in the test. The applied physicochemical 
manipulations on leachates are activated carbon, EDTA, and non-filtration. The non-filtration 
manipulation is performed in two concentration percentages of 25 and 100. The other two TIEs 
(activated carbon and EDTA) are diluted with M7 media to a leachate concentration of 25 %. All 
TIE-treated leachates are distributed into four replicates each.

4.2.1, is conducted. The new raw data can be found in Appendix B.3.

and TIEs. As seen in Figure 11, there is little response to TIE-treated leachates. Observing the 25 %

unfiltered leachates, the food-waste bag and the bio-waste bag immobilize 5 % of D. magna each. 
Moreover, the 100 % unfiltered leachates, the shopping bag, the grocery carrier bag, and the 
conventional waste bag immobilize 5 % of D. magna each, while the bio-waste bag immobilizes 20 
% of D. magna. Oddly, the 25 % food-waste bag leachate impacts D. magna, but the 100 % leachate 
does not. 
The activated carbon-treated shopping bag and food-waste bag leachates immobilize 10 % of D. 
magna each. The EDTA-treated conventional waste bag leachate immobilizes 5 % of D. magna at 24 
hours, while the EDTA-treated bio-waste bag leachate immobilizes 5 % of D. magna at 48 hours. 
4.2.3 Extra control of activated carbon, EDTA, and filtered Milli-Q water 
Controls with activated carbon, EDTA, and filtered Milli-Q water are performed to ensure that they 
are not the toxic components that immobilize D. magna but the leachates that do so. Control with 
activated carbon is produced by adding activated carbon in a flask with Milli-Q water, which was 
shaken overnight. Control with EDTA is produced by adding it to Milli-Q water. Milli-Q water 
control is to determine if Agilent Technologies’ filters release any toxic components into the water 
and thus impact D. magna. Before distribution into beakers, the controls have the M7 salts added as 
done in section 3.2.3 under “Test solutions”. Each control has four replicates, and each beaker has 5 
D. magna added as done in an acute toxicity test, and they are also performed likewise. 
No D. magna are immobilized in any of the controls. The results can be found in Appendix B.4. 
4.3 Third acute toxicity test with methanol as solvent 
The third acute toxicity test is applied to try to extract more metals and organic chemicals from the 
three (bio)plastics: the shopping bag, the bio-waste bag, and the conventional waste bag. The method 
is described in section 3.3. Only EDTA is used in the TIE. The raw data for the acute toxicity test is 
found in Appendix C. 
4.3.1 Acute toxicity test with methanol and DMSO 
The acute toxicity test was conducted from Marts 19th to April 5th, 2024. All concentrations, 10 mg 
(bio)pl./mL EDTA-TIE, and DMSO control have five replicates. The ordinary control (M7 media) 
has four replicates, while the 2.5 mg (bio)pl./mL EDTA-TIE has three replicates. A total volume of 
20 mL is used in all test beakers. Due to the few offspring of D. magna per day, the test was split into 
four parts over two weeks. 
The pH and O2 concentrations for controls, all the 10 mg (bio)pl./mL test solutions, and all TIEs were 
stable from the start to the end. The values do not deviate from the criteria described in section 3.3.1 
under “Validity of test”. 
The outcome of the acute toxicity test yields unremarkable results. There are no significant impacts 
as no D. magna are immobilized across all concentrations or EDTA-TIEs. Only the 2.5 mg 
(bio)pl./mL shopping bag extract, the 2.5 and 1.25 mg (bio)pl./mL bio-waste bag extracts, and the 10 
and 0.156 mg (bio)pl./mL conventional waste bag extracts immobilize 5 % of D. magna each. 
==PAGE:50==
 
50
Meanwhile, only the 10 mg (bio)pl./mL EDTA-treated bio-waste bag extract immobilizes 5 % of D. 
magna. The ordinary controls and DMSO control do not immobilize any D. magna. 
4.4 Analyses of the (bio)plastic leachates 
Analyses of metal, carbon, and nitrogen of the (bio)plastic leachates are conducted. Additionally, a 
non-targeted analysis is done. Data from these analyses can be found in Appendix F. The EC50/LC50 
data for metals on D. magna can be found in Appendix H. Python scripts to the EC50 of metals on D. 
magna and the metal concentrations of the (bio)plastic leachates can be found in Appendix J. 
4.4.1 Metal analyses 
Metal analyses are conducted on the unfiltered and filtered 10-day shaken (bio)plastic leachates and 
the methanol/DMSO extracted (bio)plastics. There are differences in metal concentrations between 
the unfiltered, the filtered, and the extracts. The filtered and the extracts have some metals with high 
concentrations that may impact D. magna, as seen in Figures 13 and 14. In contrast, the unfiltered 
leachates do not show any significant concentrations of metals, as seen in Figure 12. 
The filtered shopping bag and bio-waste bag leachates exceed the EC50 limit for what D. magna can 
tolerate copper (Cu). The lowest found EC50 limit for copper that D. magna can tolerate is 2 µg/L, 
while the measured copper concentrations of the shopping bag and bio-waste bag are 38.66 and 29.95 
µg/L, respectively. In the (bio)plastic extracts, all three applied (bio)plastics have copper that exceeds 
the EC50 limits, where the measured copper concentrations of the shopping bag, the bio-waste bag, 
and the conventional waste bag are 15.90, 15.92, and 74.77 µg/L, respectively. On the other hand, no 
detectable levels of copper have been measured in the unfiltered shopping bag and bio-waste bag 
leachates or in blank samples (Figure 12 and Table 5). 
Another metal with a high concentration in the (bio)plastic leachates and extracts is zinc (Zn) (Figure 
14). The lowest EC50 limit found for zinc on D. magna is 68 µg/L. None of the (bio)plastic leachates 
exceed the limit but are near it. In contrast, the shopping bag and the bio-waste bag extracts exceed 
the limit twofold, while the conventional waste bag extract exceeds almost sixfold. Comparing the 
filtered leachates to the unfiltered leachates, the filtered has higher concentrations of zinc than the 
unfiltered, except for the conventional waste bag. 
Considering the blank samples, some metals have a higher concentration in the filtered sample 
compared to the unfiltered (Figures 12 and 13). Iron (Fe), zinc (Zn), strontium (Sr), and molybdenum 
(Mo) are among them. For example, iron and zinc are under detection limits in the unfiltered sample, 
but small amounts in the filtered sample are detected. Vanadium shows a lower concentration after 
filtration, while chromium has almost the same concentration. All other metals are under the detection 
limit. 
The blank sample for the (bio)plastic extracts also has a high iron and zinc concentration. Oddly, the 
blank has the highest selenium (Se) concentration compared to the other extracts with (bio)plastic. 
The blank extract contains 25.17 µg Se/L, while the (bio)plastic extracts contain below 13.00 µg 
Se/L. The other blank samples of the unfiltered and filtered do not contain selenium. 
==PAGE:51==

on D. magna. The EC50 values can be found in Appendix H.

Regarding the rare metals, niobium (Nb), tin (Sn), and tungsten (W) are detected. Niobium is found 
in small amounts in the unfiltered food-waste bag leachate. Meanwhile, tungsten is detected in the 
shopping bag (only filtered), the food-waste bag, the bio-waste bag, and the grocery carrier bag 
leachates. The unfiltered grocery carrier bag leachate exhibits the highest concentration of tungsten 
at 71.21 µg/L. A small amount of tin is found in the conventional waste bag extract.

4.4.2 Overall metal concentrations for (bio)plastic leachates and extracts 
An overall metal concentration for all five unfiltered and filtered (bio)plastic leachates, as well as 
extracts of the shopping bag, the bio-waste bag, and the conventional waste bag, are constructed in 
Figure 15. 
==PAGE:53==
 
53
The extracts have the highest overall measured metal concentrations, especially for the conventional 
waste bag extract, which is almost sixfold from the next highest overall concentration, which also is 
an extract. Zinc is the utmost part of the concentrations of the shopping bag and the bio-waste bag 
extracts, while iron, copper, zinc, and strontium are for the conventional waste bag extract. 
 
Figure 15: Overall metal concentrations for the unfiltered and filtered (bio)plastic leachates, as well as extracts of the shopping bag, 
the bio-waste bag, and the conventional waste bag. The conventional waste bag extract has exceeded the scale almost sixfold and, 
thus, is not visible as the entirety. The overall metal concentration for the conventional waste bag extract is at the top of the bar. 
Looking at the overall concentration of metals for the leachates, the unfiltered and filtered grocery 
carrier bag leach the highest concentration, and this is due to very high concentrations of molybdenum 
(Mo) and tungsten compared to other leachates. Ignoring the concentrations of molybdenum and 
tungsten from the grocery carrier bag leachate, the overall concentration of metals is the same level 
as the other (bio)plastic leachates, except for the shopping bag and the filtered bio-waste bag. 
Additionally, if the grocery carrier bag leachate is ignored, the filtered shopping bag leaches the 
highest overall metal concentration, followed by the unfiltered shopping bag and then the filtered bio-
waste bag. 
4.4.3 Carbon and nitrogen analysis 
Carbon and nitrogen analysis is conducted for the unfiltered and filtered 10- and 20-day shaken 
(bio)plastic leachates. Total organic carbon (TOC), inorganic carbon (IC), total carbon (TC), and total 
nitrogen (TN) are measured in the leachates. The data can be found in Appendix F.2. 
The highest total carbon concentrations are detected in the unfiltered and filtered 10- and 20-day 
shaken shopping bag leachates (Figure 16). It is closely followed by the food-waste bag leachates, 
which have high TC concentrations in the unfiltered and filtered 20-day shaken leachates and half as 
much of the concentrations in the unfiltered and filtered 10-day shaken leachates. Meanwhile, the 
other (bio)plastic leachates have sparse concentrations of TC of a maximum of 20 mg C/L. Moreover, 
low nitrogen concentrations are detected in all (bio)plastic leachates (Appendix F.2).

A non-targeted analysis is conducted, where the highest total features in a (bio)plastic leachate with

waste bag, and the conventional waste bag.

4.5 Chronic toxicity test 
The chronic toxicity test is conducted as a continuation of the three acute toxicity tests. The test is 
applied to determine if bioplastic leachates cause chronic effects on D. magna. The chronic toxicity 
test was conducted from April 17th to May 8th, 2024. The chronic toxicity test is based on the OECD 
guideline no. 211. Two bioplastics are used: the shopping bag and the bio-waste bag. The two

bioplastics are described in section 3.1. The method is described in section 3.4. Three test 
concentrations of 25, 12.5, and 6.25 % are applied in a series with one bioplastic, with ten replicates 
for each concentration percentage. The bioplastics are shaken in Milli-Q for 20 days. The duration of 
the chronic test is 21 days. The microscope images of the D. magna were taken on May 17th, 2014. 
4.5.1 pH and O2 concentration during the chronic test 
During the chronic test, the pH in all concentrations for bioplastics and control was always around 
the M7 media of a pH of 8.2. If the pH of one test solution deviated from +/- 0.3, its pH was adjusted 
with acid and base as close as possible to a pH of 8.2. The pH and O2 concentrations met the criteria 
for validity. The O2 concentrations in all test concentrations and the control were more than 3 mg 
O2/L throughout the test. The pH in all test concentrations and the control did not deviate more than 
±1.5 units from the pH of the M7 media. 
 
Figure 18: The average pH and O2 concentrations of all the test concentrations for the shopping bag and the food-waste bag 
leachates during the chronic test. 
The average values for pH and O2 concentration measured during the chronic test are displayed in 
Figure 18. Compared with the control, the O2 concentrations for 12.5 and 25 % shopping bag 
leachates had decreased by 0.18 and 0.14 mg O2/L. In contrast, the O2 concentrations for 6.25 % 
shopping bag leachate and all % of the bio-waste bag leachates increased. The highest deviation of 
the O2 concentration compared to the control was the 6.25 % bio-waste bag leachate with 0.20 mg 
O2/L. 
Regarding pH, both bioplastics increased in pH compared to the control. The highest and lowest 
increase in pH was the 25 % bio-waste bag leachate (from 7.63 to 8.05) and the 6.25 % shopping bag 
leachate (from 7.63 to 7.70). Overall, the bio-waste bag leachate increased in pH more than the 
shopping bag leachate compared to the control. 
4.5.2 Cumulative parent mortality of D. magna 
The mortalities of the parent D. magna were observed during the chronic test. If a parent animal died 
and had offspring, they were noted, but the replicate was excluded from the assessment. The test 
failed to meet the criteria for validity, as at the end of the test, the cumulative parent mortality per test

concentrations of 6.25 and 25 % had cumulative parent mortalities of 50 and 30 %, respectively. The 
first mortality occurred on day 1 as the parent D. magna accidentally dried on the surface of the test 
beaker. Besides the accidental mortality, the first mortality occurred on day 5.

bioplastic leachates, but some patterns could be observed. The same applies to the number of molts, 
where the range was spread between 3 to 5 molts per parent D. magna.

Molts of the control group 
Four replicates only received up to the 3rd molt (Table 6). 
Three of the replicates died on the day the 3rd molt came. Six 
replicates received the 4th molt and were still alive at the end 
of the test, and one replicate received the 5th molt. There is no 
clear pattern of when the same molt came across the control 
replicates. The shortest and longest span between the 1st and 
4th molt is 9 and 10 days. The span between the 1st molt and 
the 5th molt is 13 days. 
Molts of the shopping bag leachate 
Seven replicates of the 6.25 % leachate received the 4th molt 
on the second to the last test day (Table 7). Also, two replicates 
received the 5th molt. No replicates of the 6.25 % stopped to 
the 3rd molt. In the 12.5 % leachate, one replicate only received 
up to the 2nd molt and died. Three replicates only received up 
to the 3rd molt, which came very late on days 18-20. Four 
replicates received the 4th molt, while one received the 5th molt. 
The 25 % leachate had no 5th molts. Three and six replicates 
received up to the 3rd and 4th molts, respectively. 
There was no clear pattern of when the same molts came across 
the concentrations of the shopping bag leachate, except for the 
25 %, in which each molt almost came simultaneously. The colors lined up on days 12-13, 15-16, 18-
20, and 21 for the 1st, 2nd, 3rd, and 4th molt, respectively.  
Molts of the bio-waste bag leachate 
Five replicates of the 6.25 % received up to the 3rd molts very late on days 18-19 (Table 8). Three of 
them died on the day or shortly after the molt came. Only one replicate received the 4th molt, while 
two received the 5th molt. In the 12.5 %, two replicates received the 3rd molt on day 18 and died, while 
one replicate received the 3rd molt on day 19 and died two days later, on the last day of the test. Four 
and one replicate(s) received the 4th and 5th molt, respectively, on the last day. One replicate of 25 % 
only received up to the 3rd molt and was still alive on the last day of the test. Five and one replicate(s) 
received the 4th and 5th molt, respectively. 
There was a somewhat clear pattern of when the same molts came in the 25 % bio-waste leachate. 
Meanwhile, the 12.5 % had a clear pattern, which the color lined up on days 11-12, 14-15, 18-19, and 
21 for the 1st, 2nd, 3rd, and 4th molt, respectively. 6.25 % had no pattern, and all molts were spread on 
different days.

Time to the 1st molt and the average days until the subsequent molts 
The control groups received the 1st molts on days 8-9 and 11-12 at the earliest and the latest, 
respectively. The control group had an average of 3-4 days between molts. 
==PAGE:59==
 
59
The 6.25 and 12.5 % shopping bag leachates received the 1st molts on days 8-10 and 11-13 at the 
earliest and the latest, respectively. On average, the 1st molts came late on days 12-13. Moreover, the 
25 % leachate received the 1st molts very late on days 12-13. The average days between molts for the 
shopping bag was 3-4 days. 
Regarding bio-waste bag leachate, one 1st molt from each concentration stood out because these three 
molts came on days 8-9. Otherwise, all molts arrived very late compared to the control group. The 
“next” 1st molts first arrived on days 11-12. One replicate of the 6.25 % received the 1st molt on day 
13. The average days between molts for the bio-waste bag was 3-4 days, with one replicate each from 
the 6.25 and 25 % that had an interval of 2 days between molts. 
4.5.4 Cumulative offspring per parent D. magna 
The number of offspring per parent D. magna were noted daily. The total sets of data can be found 
in Appendices D.1, D.2, and D.3 for control, the shopping bag leachate, and the bio-waste bag 
leachate, respectively. The average cumulative offspring per parent D. magna per day for each 
bioplastic leachate in all concentrations and control are displayed in Appendices D.2, Figure 29, and 
D.3, Figure 30. The test met the criteria for validity, as the mean number of living offspring per parent 
D. magna across all test concentrations and the control was more than 60 at the end of the test. 
A one-factorial ANOVA analysis was conducted to see if there was a significant difference in the 
average cumulative offspring per parent D. magna between four groups (control, 6.25, 12.5, and 25 
% leachate) for one bioplastic leachate. The null hypothesis is that the average cumulative offspring 
per parent D. magna in the four groups is statistically identical and is tested against the alternative 
hypothesis (Appendix D.7, Table 68).  
 
Figure 21: Cumulative offspring per parent D. magna exposed to shopping bag leachate in 21 days. 1: Control group, 2: 6.25 %, 3: 
12.5 %, and 4: 25 %. Groups that do not share the same letter are significantly different from each other. 
The null hypothesis for the bio-waste bag leachate is accepted, meaning that the average cumulative 
offspring per parent D. magna in the four groups is statistically identical. However, the null 
hypothesis for the shopping bag leachate is rejected. Therefore, post hoc comparisons are needed, 
where the pairwise hypothesis tests investigate which groups are different (Appendix D.7, Table 69). 
The tests show that the average cumulative offspring per parent D. magna for control significantly 
differs from all three groups containing the shopping bag leachate (Figure 21). Moreover, there were 
no significant differences across the three groups containing the leachate, so the null hypotheses are 
accepted. Based on the conclusion of the post hoc tests, the No Observed Effect Concentration 
==PAGE:60==
 
60
(NOEC) for the shopping bag leachate is not identified. Meanwhile, the 6.25 % concentration of the 
leachate is identified as Lowest Observed Effect Concentration (LOEC). 
4.5.5 Average lengths of parent D. magna 
The lengths of parent D. magna were measured after 21 days of exposure to the bioplastic leachates 
(Figure 22). The lengths of the control group were also measured. The average lengths of parent D. 
magna in different concentration percentages and control are almost identical, with a slight difference 
of 0.27 mm between the largest and the smallest average length. 
 
Figure 22: Average length of parent D. magna and control group after 21 days of exposure to bioplastic leachates. The average lengths 
are shown together with the standard deviation of each test concentration. 
The ANOVA analysis was again used, where the null hypothesis is that the average length of parent 
D. magna in the four groups (control, 6.25, 12.5, and 25 % leachate) is statistically identical and is 
tested against the alternative hypothesis (Appendix D.7, Table 70). The null hypotheses for both 
bioplastic leachates are accepted, meaning that the average length per parent D. magna in the four 
groups is statistically identical in both leachates. 
4.5.6 Average lengths of 4th molt offspring 
The lengths of 4th molts offspring of D. magna were also measured after 21 days of exposure to the 
bioplastic leachates (Figure 23). 
The effects of bioplastic leachates on the average lengths of offspring vary across concentration 
percentages. An ANOVA analysis was conducted, and the null hypothesis is that the average length 
of the 4th molt offspring in the four groups is statistically identical and is tested against the alternative 
hypothesis (Appendix D.7, Table 71). The null hypotheses for both bioplastic leachates are rejected 
since the average lengths of offspring in the four groups are statistically different in both leachates. 
Consequently, post hoc comparisons with the pairwise hypothesis tests are applied (Appendix D.7, 
Tables 72 and 73). 
Concerning the shopping bag leachate, the lengths of offspring in the 6.25 and 25 % leachates are 
significantly different from the length of offspring in the control group (Figure 24a). In contrast, the

the 25 % is LOEC. These are identified centered on the fact that 12.5 % is significantly identical to 
the control group, meaning there is no effect. In comparison, 25 % is significantly different from the 
control group, meaning there is an effect.

4.6 Shopping bag and bio-waste leachates under microscope 
The unfiltered 20-day shaken shopping bag and bio-waste bag leachates from the chronic toxicity test 
were examined for microplastics and other pollutants under a microscope. This was conducted by 
taking one drop from a thoroughly shaken flask containing bioplastic leachate with a pipette. The one 
small randomly selected drop of the leachate was transferred onto a glass, which was investigated 
under the microscope. 
The shopping bag leachate had a lot of debris, which can be observed in Figures 25a, b, and c. A lot 
of the debris was tiny, but some were found in size between 29-336 m. Conversely, the bio-waste 
bag leachate did not have as much debris, but when found, they were very tiny, with a maximal size 
of 60 m (Figures 26a, b, and c).

5 Discussion 
This section discusses the results from the acute and chronic toxicity tests and analyses on the 
(bio)plastic leachates. First, the content of (bio)plastic leachates and extracts is discussed, where the 
analyses of metals, carbon, nitrogen, number of features, and microplastic are assessed. Secondly, the 
acute toxicity assessments of the (bio)plastic leachates and extracts, together with the TIEs, are 
discussed. Next, the chronic toxicity assessment of two bioplastic leachates is discussed. 
Additionally, the toxicity of the shopping bag and the bio-waste bag leachates is rationalized, where, 
case by case, they are considered. Finally, the sources of errors and uncertainties are considered, and 
hence future recommendations are constituted. 
5.1 Analyses of the (bio)plastic leachates and extracts 
Analyses have proven that both metals and organic chemicals can and may have some impacts on D. 
magna (section 4.4). This section will discuss the degree of impact metals or organic chemicals can 
have on D. magna. The table below shows an overview of the quantity of different components 
(metals, organic carbon, and features) in the (bio)plastic leachates (Table 9). Only the overall 
concentration of metals was measured for (bio)plastic extracts. 
Table 9: Overview of the quantity of metals, organic carbon, and features. The symbols indicate the size of the quantity ranging from 
low to high: -, +, ++, +++.

5.1.1 Metal toxicity 
Some metals in the (bio)plastic leachates and extracts are proven to be high enough to impact D. 
magna (section 4.4.1, Figure 13). According to the overall concentration of metals, the (bio)plastic 
extracts have almost sixfold more concentrations of metals extracted compared to how much the 
==PAGE:64==
 
64
(bio)plastic leachates have the metals leached (section 4.4.2, Figure 15). However, not all metals 
exceed the EC50 limitation in concentration for how much D. magna can tolerate these metals. 
If comparing the unfiltered and filtered (bio)plastic leachates, it is clear that the filtered leachates 
have higher overall concentrations of metal than the unfiltered, except for the grocery carrier bag 
(section 4.4.1, Figures 12 and 13). The indication is also proven by the fact that some metal 
concentrations in the filtered blank sample are higher than in the unfiltered sample (section 4.4.1, 
Table 5). As mentioned in section 3.2.2, filters are used on leachates in order from the most filters 
used to the least: Shopping bag > Food-waste bag = Bio-waste bag > Grocery carrier bag = 
Conventional waste bag. This also demonstrates that filtration influences the metal concentrations in 
the leachates since the least filters used on leachates is the grocery carrier bag, and the unfiltered one 
has a higher concentration of metals than the filtered one. 
Apart from the fact that the filtrations influence the metal concentrations in the leachates, none of the 
unfiltered leachates exceeds the EC50 limitations, but filtered leachates do (section 4.4.1, Figure 13). 
As the filtered leachates are used in the acute toxicity tests, some metals, therefore, individually can 
impact D. magna. Copper and zinc are the metals that may impact D. magna since copper 
concentrations in the shopping bag and the bio-waste bag leachates exceed the EC50 limit, and zinc 
concentrations in all (bio)plastic leachates are near the limit. All the (bio)plastic extracts exceed the 
EC50 limitations for copper and zinc (section 4.4.1, Figure 14). 
Copper is a particular case since the filtered shopping bag and bio-waste bag leachates contain copper, 
but the unfiltered ones and both unfiltered and filtered blank samples do not (section 4.4.1, Table 5 
and Figures 12 and 13). Also, the three (bio)plastic extracts contain copper, but the blank extract does 
not. This suggests that the shopping bag and the bio-waste bag do, in fact, contain copper, as the 
extracts contain copper. The metal either does not readily migrate into water or does originate from 
the filters applied. It is not entirely clear where copper comes from, but the copper released from the 
filters is proven not to affect D. magna, as an extra control with filtered Milli-Q water and added M7 
salts was conducted (section 4.2.3). The control test demonstrates that no toxic substances released 
from Agilent Technologies’ filters into the water are high enough to impact D. magna, as none of 
them was immobilized.  
5.1.2 Organic chemicals toxicity and number of features in leachates 
According to the carbon analysis, the (bio)plastic leachates, from the highest to the lowest 
concentrations of carbon, range from: shopping bag > food-waste bag > bio-waste bag > conventional 
waste bag = grocery carrier bag (section 4.4.3, Figure 16). This is in accordance with the fact that the 
shopping bag and the food-waste bag are biobased. Also, because they are biodegradable, it may be 
easier to fragment and leach more carbon and chemicals into water compared to other bioplastics or 
conventional plastics. This is also demonstrated by the fact that the food-waste bag leached more 
carbon if allowed to stay in the water for ten more days. Additionally, since the bio-waste bag is bio-
based but not biodegradable, it may explain why there is less carbon in the bio-waste bag leachate. It 
could also be because it contains less biomaterial than the other two bioplastics. Furthermore, the 
conventional waste bag and the grocery carrier bag leachates have sparse concentrations of carbon of 
==PAGE:65==
 
65
a maximum of 20 mg C/L, which is in accordance with the fact that they are neither biobased nor 
biodegradable, suggesting it takes a long time to fragment and leach the chemicals into water. 
Moreover, the carbon and nitrogen analyses were not conducted on the (bio)plastic extracted with 
methanol and DMSO since the extracts contain methanol, which can be combusted during analysis. 
Therefore, a comparison between (bio)plastic leachates and extracts cannot be achieved and 
discussed. 
Non-targeted analysis 
Further, a non-targeted analysis was conducted on the five (bio)plastic leachates, and they, from the 
most to fewest features, range from: shopping bag > food-waste bag > grocery carrier bag  bio-waste 
bag > conventional waste bag (section 4.4.4, Figure 17). Interestingly, the leachates containing most 
features stem from biobased and biodegradable bags, and the non-biodegradable bio-waste bag 
contains almost as many features as the conventional plastic grocery carrier bag. This is in accordance 
with the fact that biobased and biodegradable bioplastics can and may readily biodegrade and contain 
more additives than conventional plastics. 
Zimmerman et al. (2020a) conducted a non-targeted analysis across 43 bioplastics and plant-based 
materials and compared them with conventional plastics. The total number of chemical features 
detected for biobased and biodegradable plastics is between 614 and 17,224, while for non-biobased 
but biodegradable plastics is 3864 and 15,843. The total number of features for conventional plastics 
was between 0 and 194, as concluded in their previous study [Zimmermann et al., 2019], which means 
that the bioplastics contain a more complex mixture of chemicals. Additionally, they analyzed 
whether bio-based and/or biodegradable plastics contain fewer toxic chemicals than conventional 
plastics, and they concluded that bioplastics were just as toxic as they induced baseline toxicity in the 
in vitro bioassay with bacterium Aliivibrio fischeri. Also, when investigating whether features were 
shared among multiple samples per material, it was clear that about half of all features detected in 
PLA were unique to one sample. Again, this demonstrates that bioplastics can and may contain more 
additives than conventional plastics. 
It should be noted that non-targeting analysis cannot be directly compared as instruments and settings 
can and may differ across studies. For example, Zimmermann et al. (2019 and 2020a) may have 
detected more features using a different data analysis strategy. Also, Zimmermann et al. (2020a) argue 
that the choice of extraction of plastic and bioplastic can and may be different and influence the 
composition of detected chemicals. For example, Zimmermann et al. (2019 and 2020a) extracted the 
content of the plastics and bioplastics with the help of methanol and DMSO, while the plastics and 
bioplastics were water-extracted, so the content was leached into water, which was used for the non-
targeting. 
5.1.3 Microplastics in bioplastic leachates 
The shopping bag and the bio-waste bag were investigated for microplastics under a microscope 
(section 4.6, Figures 25 and 26). The shopping bag leachate has some microplastic, whereas the bio-
waste bag has few. As the shopping bag is biodegradable, and with the fact that biodegradable 
==PAGE:66==
 
66
bioplastics may be more easily degraded into microplastics, it makes sense that the shopping bag has 
fragmented. However, the question is whether the microplastics contribute to the toxicity on D. 
magna. The microplastics can contribute to the toxicity on D. magna chemically or physically. 
Chemically, the additives or other environmental pollutants adsorbed on the microplastics can be 
released in D. magna, while physically, the microplastics can cause system obstruction. 
The plastic and bioplastic leachates used in the acute toxicity test were filtered with 0.45 and/or 0.22 
µm filters. According to Hartmann et al. (2019), the size of the particles that may escape the filtration 
is characterized as nanoplastics, which have a size range between 1 nm to 1 µm. This means that the 
filtration may have removed microplastics, but not nanoplastics, and whether an impact nanoplastics 
have on the toxicity on D. magna is not certain. The non-filtration TIEs are discussed below in section 
5.3.2. 
5.2 Acute toxicity assessments of the (bio)plastic leachates
