Based on the preliminary and second acute toxicity tests (sections 4.1.1 and 4.2.1), the shopping bag 
and bio-waste bag exhibit toxicity that can immobilize D. magna (Table 10). The 10-day shaken 
(bio)plastic leachates did not produce any effect concentrations in the preliminary acute test, as they 
==PAGE:67==
 
67
immobilized no more than 15 % of D. magna for one concentration percentage. Furthermore, the 
shopping bag, the bio-waste bag, and the grocery carrier bag were proven more toxic among the 
(bio)plastics, but the toxicities are still negligible. 
In the second acute test, only the shopping bag and bio-waste bag were toxic enough to yield effect 
concentrations; thus, 24- and 48-hour concentration-response curves were produced for both 
leachates (Figures 9 and 10). However, the EC50 for the shopping bag leachate at 24 hours is 176±4.75 
%, which is well above 100 % leachate concentration, so the result can not conclude anything. 
Nonetheless, the 48-hr EC50 for the shopping bag is 76±0.12 %. Furthermore, the bio-waste bag 
leachate has 24- and 48-hr EC50 of 85±0.29 and 68±0.014 %. Comparing the 48-hr EC50 for both 
leachates, the shopping bag is more toxic than the bio-waste bag. 
Besides the two (bio)plastics discussed, the other two (bio)plastic leachates of the food-waste bag and 
the conventional waste bag exhibit minimal toxicity. Nevertheless, it is more toxic than in the 
preliminary test. The toxicity of the grocery carrier bag is consistent both in the 10- and 20-day 
leachates. 
By comparing the 10- and 20-day leachates, the toxicity is more pronounced in the leachates that 
have been shaken-stirred the longest, especially for bioplastics rather than conventional plastics. This 
suggests that if the longer (bio)plastic remains in the water, where the water washes through the 
matrix of the (bio)plastic, the potentially further toxic leachate becomes to D. magna. 
5.3 Toxicity identification evaluations of the (bio)plastic leachates 
The TIEs in acute toxicity tests were applied to pinpoint which component in (bio)plastic leachates 
is harmful (sections 4.1.2, 4.2.2, and 4.3.1). 
5.3.1 Activated carbon and EDTA 
The EDTA-TIEs in the preliminary test show that the shopping bag and bio-waste bag leachates have 
the greatest impact on the D. magna, with 100 % immobilization in both leachates at the end of the 
test (section 4.1.2, Figure 8). However, in the second and third tests, neither the shopping bag and 
bio-waste bag leachates nor other (bio)plastic leachates have any impact on D. magna (section 4.2.2, 
Figure 11 and section 4.3.1). 
The difference between the preliminary and second tests may be due to a change in pH, different 
speciation of metals, or more bioavailable dissolved carbon is present. However, the difference 
between the pH, the metals and their concentrations, and the carbon content are minimal (Table 10). 
It could also be that the EDTA was not allowed to chelate with the metals for a while to complex, and 
the EDTA-treated leachates were immediately used for the toxicity test. Consequently, some metals 
were not rendered non-toxic due to a slow complexation rate. 
In the preliminary test, due to the slow complexation of metals or the influence of pH, the shopping 
bag and the bio-waste bag leachates exhibit metal toxicity on D. magna, as neither of the leachates 
affects D. magna in the second test. Otherwise, it can be explained that the organic chemicals do, in 
fact, impact D. magna in the preliminary test but not in the second. 
==PAGE:68==
 
68
The TIEs with activated carbon tell a different version since D. magna is only minimally impacted 
by the conventional waste bag leachate in the preliminary test, while other leachates do not affect D. 
magna at all. However, the result of the conventional waste bag is concluded on the fact that only one 
test beaker has all its D. magna immobilized, while other beakers have no immobilized D. magna. 
Additionally, in the second test, the shopping bag and the food-waste bag leachates have minimal 
impact on D. magna. If the activated carbon in fact absorbs all organic chemicals and only metals are 
left in the leachates, the metal toxicity in the mentioned (bio)plastic leachates is true. The activated 
carbon was also allowed to be shaken-stirred longer in the leachate (overnight), compared to EDTA, 
which was used immediately when spiked into the leachates. 
5.3.2 Non-filtration/Filtration 
The non-filtration TIEs may conclude that the viscous (bio)plastic leachates do not contain some 
toxicity that would otherwise have been filtered out, such as some metals, microplastics, and particles. 
However, if micro/nanoplastics or other particles were filtered out, the adsorbed metals and organic 
chemicals could be released and become more biologically available, thus more toxic to D. magna. 
On the other hand, D. magna are still exposed to these toxicants by ingesting them. Therefore, 
removing particles may cause toxicity or reduce it. 
Due to the filtration of the (bio)plastic leachates, which have influenced the overall concentrations of 
metals, the filtered leachates may exhibit more toxicity than the unfiltered. Some filtered leachates 
also have metal concentrations that exceed the EC50 limit or are near it. However, this is not the case 
as almost none of the unfiltered (bio)plastic leachates, both at 25 and 100 % concentrations, impact 
D. magna. Only the grocery carrier bag leachate impacts D. magna in the preliminary test, while only 
the 100 % bio-waste bag leachate does in the second test. 
During the filtration of the leachates, the pH can be altered, which can affect the speciation of the 
metals or change ionic or polar organic chemicals into non-ionic or non-polar forms in the leachates, 
but this is not the case, as the pH was the same as the unfiltered leachates with a maximal deviation 
of one unit. 
5.4 Acute toxicity assessments of the (bio)plastic extracts and EDTA-TIEs 
The third acute toxicity test with the (bio)plastic extracts proved as not toxic to D. magna, with very 
minimal toxicity of the highest concentration of the bio-waste bag extract (10 mg (bio)pl./mL), which 
immobilized 8 % of D. magna (2 of 25) after 48 hours (section 4.3.1). So, interestingly, it may not be 
the toxicity of the metals that impacts D. magna since the three (bio)plastic extracts contain far more 
zinc and even far more overall metal concentrations than the (bio)plastic leachates. The extracts 
exceed the EC50 limit for the copper and zinc but do not particularly impact D. magna. 
Only the TIE with EDTA has been applied for the (bio)plastic extracts, so only organic chemicals 
may influence the result. None of the extracts exhibit organic chemicals toxicity on D. magna. This 
suggests that neither the metals nor organic chemicals do not contribute to the toxicity on D. magna, 
at least for the (bio)plastic extracts. 
==PAGE:69==
 
69
As only metals analysis was conducted on the extracts, the carbon and nitrogen content, as well as 
chemical features, are unknown. However, Zimmermann et al. (2021) conducted a study with 
extractable and leachable chemicals from plastics and bioplastics. Here, they concluded that the 
extractable and leachable chemicals are not the same, as not all extractable chemicals are leaching 
and/or that the concentration of the leachable chemicals is lower than that of the extractable ones. 
Zimmermann et al. (2021) also argue that leachable chemicals can serve as an indicator of the 
chemical toxicity readily released in conditions commonly encountered after disposal. Additionally, 
the leachable chemicals from PLA bioplastic did induce baseline toxicity. This emphasizes the 
importance and the need for toxicity testing the complex leachates, and it is preferred under realistic 
environmental conditions, e.g., in the aquatic environment, when the bioplastics are in contact with 
water, they can and may slowly leach substances into the environment. 
5.5 Chronic toxicity assessment of two bioplastic leachates 
The chronic toxicity assessment did not meet the validity criterion on cumulative mortality of the 
control group, as more than 20 % of parent D. magna died during the test period (section 4.5). Besides 
that, the other validity criteria were fulfilled. The pH in all test concentrations and the control did not 
deviate more than ±1.5 units from the pH of the M7 media during the test period (section 4.5.1, Figure 
18). There may be a minimal probability that pH fluctuations can affect the speciation of the metals 
or change ionic or polar organic chemicals into non-ionic or non-polar forms in the leachates. 
Table 11: Overview of the effects of the bioplastic leachates on D. magna in the chronic toxicity assessment. The cumulative 
mortality of parent D. magna is in percentage. If one cell contains two %, the first % is without parent D. magna, which died on the 
last day of the test, and the second % is with parent D. magna, which died on the last day of the test. The null hypothesis (H0) is either 
accepted or rejected on the grounds that one group containing bioplastic leachate is either statistically identical or different from the 
control group.

5.5.1 Cumulative parent D. magna mortality 
Looking at the cumulative parent D. magna mortalities across concentration percentages (section 
4.5.2), the lower percentages have more cumulative mortalities than the highest concentration 
percentage (Table 11). Why this is so is mysterious. It could be considered that, as the leachates were 
==PAGE:70==
 
70
unfiltered, a lower leachate concentration gives fewer microplastics and particles, meaning there may 
be more bioavailable organic chemicals to D. magna. In comparison, a higher leachate concentration 
may contain less bioavailable organic chemicals. At the same time, the leachates are mixed with the 
M7 media, which encompasses a little EDTA, so that the metals may be chelated with the EDTA. So, 
if a low leachate concentration, e.g., 6.25 %, is mixed with a lot of M7 media, most of the metals may 
be chelated with the EDTA and, thus, may exhibit less metal toxicity. In contrast, when a high 
leachate concentration is mixed with less M7 media, only a few metals may be chelated. 
The 12.5 % leachate of both bioplastics has a higher cumulative parent D. magna mortality than the 
other two concentrations, possibly due to a surplus of the bioavailable organic chemicals and metals 
that are finely balanced between each other. 
According to the cumulative parent mortality of D. magna, the bio-waste bag leachate exhibits higher 
toxicity than the shopping bag leachate. The question is, what does contribute to the toxicity? Looking 
at the shopping bag leachate, the greatest mortality is at 12.5 % leachate, which means both the metal 
and organic chemicals may contribute to the toxicity. Meanwhile, the greatest mortality in the bio-
waste bag is 6.25 and/or 12.5 %, which means they lean against organic chemicals that may contribute 
to toxicity, as the metals are bound to EDTA in the M7 media. There is also the possibility of factors 
other than exposure to bioplastic leachates that can influence the result, such as the fitness of D. 
magna, which cannot be clarified. 
5.5.2 Time to the 1st molt 
Although it has been described that the bio-waste bag leachate induced more toxicity than the 
shopping bag leachate, this is not reflected in the time it takes to have the 1st molt (section 4.5.3). The 
control group acquired the 1st molt on day 8, while the bioplastic leachates acquired the 1st molt on 
average very late on days 11-13. Interestingly, the average days between molts for both the shopping 
bag and the bio-waste bag leachates were 3-4 days, as well as for the control group. The delay in 
molting could be due to the impact of the bioplastic leachates or other factors, such as the fitness of 
D. magna. 
5.5.3 Statistical analyses of the average cumulative offspring per parent D. magna and 
the average lengths of parent D. magna and offspring 
The statistical one-factorial ANOVA analyses on both bioplastic leachates were conducted (sections 
4.5.4-4.5.6). Firstly, the average cumulative offspring per parent D. magna for the shopping bag 
leachate was proven to be significantly different between the control group and all three 
concentrations of the leachate (section 4.5.4, Figure 21). In comparison, the three concentrations are 
significantly identical to each other. This suggests that the shopping bag leachate affects how many 
offspring per parent D. magna acquires, and, in this case, they acquire more offspring than the control 
group. Moreover, all concentrations of the bio-waste bag leachate were proven to be significantly 
identical to the control group. 
Additionally, the average length of parent D. magna between both the shopping bag and the bio-waste 
bag leachates to the control group are statistically identical (section 4.5.5). This suggests that the 
==PAGE:71==
 
71
parent D. magna may get enough nutrition and enough to eat, and they were not affected in growth 
by the leachates. 
Lastly, the average lengths of the 4th molt offspring vary across concentration percentages in both 
bioplastic leachates (section 4.5.6, Figures 24a and b). Regarding the shopping bag leachate, the 
average lengths of offspring in the 6.25 and 25 % leachates are significantly different from those in 
the control group. In comparison, the 12.5 % is significantly identical to the control group. Moreover, 
there are no significant differences in the lengths between the three groups containing the leachate. 
Moving to the bio-waste bag leachate, the 25 % leachate is significantly different from those in the 
control group, while the 6.25 and 12.5 % leachates are significantly identical to the control group. 
Interestingly, the three groups containing the leachate among each other are also significantly 
different in the average lengths of offspring. 
Comparing the average lengths of the 4th molt offspring, the bioplastic leachates impact parent D. 
magna, so their offspring are, on average, smaller than the control group. Combining the increased 
number of offspring per parent D. magna with a decrease in the average length of offspring exposed 
to the shopping bag leachate may be explained by a reallocation of biomass. Moreover, the bio-waste 
bag leachate only affects D. magna by the average length of offspring and only by a leachate 
concentration of 25 %. 
5.5.4 Comparing with Zimmermann et al. (2020b) 
A study with a D. magna chronic toxicity test was conducted by Zimmermann et al. (2020b), where 
they charted whether the chemicals extracted or migrated from the microplastic or microplastic itself 
is the driving factor for toxicity. The chronic toxicity test was conducted in the same way as done in 
the thesis but in different exposure scenarios, including microplastics and microplastics without 
extractable chemicals, as well as the extracted and migrating chemicals alone. 
The result obtained for PLA by Zimmermann et al. (2020b) signifies that PLA microplastic reduced 
the survival of the parent D. magna with a mortality of 60 % at the highest concentration of PLA (500 
mg/L), and the reproductive output was significantly compared to the control with 21 neonates per 
surviving parent. Also, the mean length of parent D. magna was significantly small in animals 
exposed to 500 mg/L, with a difference of 0.8 mm compared to the control group. 
By using the different exposure scenarios, Zimmermann et al. (2020b) also concluded that the 
contribution of plastic chemicals to the toxicity on D. magna is plastic-type-specific. They attribute 
the effects of the conventional plastic polyvinylchloride (PVC) to the chemicals, while the 
microplastic itself induced the effects of the PLA bioplastics. 
This highlights the importance of considering microplastic and the fact that the abundance or the 
number of plastic chemicals does not predict the toxicity of bioplastic leachates or extracts. 
Zimmermann et al. (2020b) also argue that the microplastic size and shape may contribute to the 
toxicity on D. magna, which is not considered in the chronic test in this thesis. Yet, microplastics 
cannot be treated as a homogenous entity, and the chemical compositions of the microplastics also 
need to be taken into account. 
==PAGE:72==
 
72
5.6 So, what was the toxicity component in the (bio)plastics then? 
If only looking at one (bio)plastic at a time, e.g., the shopping bag (Table 12) and the bio-waste bag 
(Table 13), and evaluating the information obtained from the acute and chronic toxicity tests, as well 
as the different analyses conducted, the toxicant contributes to toxicity on D. magna may be 
identified. 
5.6.1 Shopping bag leachate 
The shopping bag contains metals with concentrations high enough to immobilize D. magna. Also, it 
contains a high concentration of organic carbon and a high number of chemical features. Additionally, 
the bag also releases microplastics. Based on the toxicity tests, the toxicant that contributes to the 
acute toxicity in the shopping bag leachate on D. magna may be metal and organic chemicals. In the 
preliminary acute test, it appears the organic chemical is the toxicant, while in the second test, the 
metals are the toxicant. If the third test is included, it eliminates metals as a toxicant. It provides the 
following conclusion: the toxicant in the shopping bag is organic chemicals. However, looking at the 
EDTA-TIEs in the third test, the organic chemicals have no effect either.

5.6.2 Bio-waste bag leachate 
In contrast to the shopping bag, the bio-waste bag has a low concentration of organic carbon and 
fewer chemical features. Also, the bag is not biobased and does not release microplastics as much as 
the shopping bag. Based on the toxicity tests, the toxicant contributing to the acute toxicity in the bio-
waste bag leachate on D. magna remains unclear. In the preliminary acute test, it appears the organic 
chemical is the toxicant based on the EDTA-TIE result, which significantly impacts D. magna. 
Meanwhile, in the second test, the desorbed metals and organic chemicals may be the toxicants. The 
conclusion is established from the fact that the filtered leachate significantly impacts D. magna, while 
in the second acute test with non-filtration-TIE, the D. magna is also impacted. This suggests that the 
microplastics may contain metals and organic chemicals that are adsorbed onto microplastics, which 
may cause toxicity when ingested by D. magna. If the third test is included, it eliminates metals as a 
toxicant, which has a minimal impact on D. magna. However, looking at the EDTA-TIEs in the third 
test, the organic chemicals have no effect.

reduced average length of offspring in the 25 % leachates. 
5.7 Sources of errors and uncertainties 
The peculiar data achieved in the EDTA-TIEs can be influenced by the M7 media. The M7 media 
contains salts that provide a suitable development of Daphnia cultures, and it contains EDTA, which 
may introduce errors in the evaluation of the toxicity of some metals. Guilhermino et al. (1997) 
conducted a study that proved that EDTA in the M7 media reduced the toxicity of some metals to D. 
magna. Therefore, if evaluating the toxicity of metals in D. magna, Guilhermino et al. (1997) 
recommend using another media, and the M7 media is only used carefully for the toxicity evaluation 
of mixtures and effluents containing metals. 
The M7 media used in the acute and chronic toxicity tests is mixed by the laboratory technician, and 
a new batch is made every week or two weeks. The media contains the same concentrations of salts, 
but the pH can vary. The laboratory technician measures and adjusts the pH of the new media every 
time so the pH can remain the same. However, the pH can sometimes deviate, and the pH needs to 
be adjusted before use for the toxicity test. 
To make the (bio)plastic leachates, the (bio)plastics are cut by scissors, which may release metals 
onto them. How much it influences may be minimal. At the same time, the cut (bio)plastics may be 
polluted when they touch the table or the weight when they have to be weighed, which may contain 
traces of contamination, such as other chemicals or salts used in the laboratory. 
5.8 Future recommendations 
Based on the obtained knowledge throughout this thesis, a list of recommendations for further 
research has been formulated. The recommendations are provided with the ambition of improving 
future laboratory tests by constructing D. magna ecotoxicity test set-ups with closer proximity to 
realistic environmental conditions. 
• Standardizing the ecotoxicity testing of bioplastic leachates 
Ecotoxicities of bioplastic leachate vary much across the environmental compartments, so 
there is a need to standardize the ecotoxicity testing to obtain comparability of the toxicity of 
the leachates. An effort is required to agree on methodologies, standards, and data sharing to 
establish common criteria and regulatory frameworks for assessing the toxicity of the 
leachates. 
• Ecotoxicity of the bioplastic leachates is significantly missing in the freshwater environment 
There is a need to conduct several more toxicity tests on bioplastic leachates, especially if 
bioplastics should replace conventional plastics in the future. Replicates of the different 
toxicity tests also help to draw more confident conclusions about whether bioplastics pose 
an environmental hazard. 
==PAGE:75==
 
75
• Improve the use of toxicity identification evaluations 
More physical-chemical manipulations should be applied to draw a better conclusion on 
which component in the complex bioplastic leachate may contribute to the toxicity on D. 
magna or other test organisms. 
• Different filters should be used 
Filters used for the filtration of leachates should not contain any metal, so the filters do not 
influence metal analysis or the toxicity test conducted. 
• Non-targeted analysis - tentatively identified the found chemical features 
The non-targeted analysis is an essential tool and is even more helpful if the found chemical 
features are tentatively identified, as done by Zimmermann et al. (2019, 2020a, and 2021), 
then the toxicant may be identified. 
• Transparency about additives applied in plastics and bioplastics is needed 
Information about the chemical composition of plastics and bioplastics is often kept 
confidential by producers. Therefore, the risk posed by the additive leaching from plastics 
and bioplastics is still unknown and challenging to chart. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:76==
 
76
6 Conclusion 
The main aim of the thesis was to investigate the ecotoxicity of bioplastic bags on the test organism 
freshwater zooplankton Daphnia magna. Three bioplastic and two conventional plastic bags were 
applied in the thesis: the shopping bag, the food-waste bag, the bio-waste bag, the conventional waste 
bag, and the grocery carrier bag. Two acute toxicity tests were conducted with 10- and 20-day water-
extraction leaching of plastics and bioplastics, and one acute toxicity test was conducted with 
methanol/DMSO-extraction. The acute toxicity tests followed the OECD guideline no. 202. 
Combined with the acute tests, the toxicity identification evaluations were applied to evaluate if the 
toxicant in leachates could be found. Additionally, one chronic toxicity test was conducted on two 
bioplastics: the shopping bag and the bio-waste bag The chronic toxicity tests followed the OECD 
guideline no. 202. Analyses of metals, carbon, nitrogen, and the number of chemical features were 
performed. 
On the basis of the results obtained and analyses, the bioplastics, the shopping bag and the bio-waste 
bag may pose an ecotoxicological effect on aquatic environments if they were wrongfully disposed 
of, as these two bags had the most significant impact on D. magna compared to other bags. The 
shopping bag leachate had a 48-hour EC50 of 76 %, while the bio-waste bag leachate had a 48-hour 
EC50 of 68 %. The other bags, the food-waste bag, the conventional waste bag, and the grocery carrier 
bag, had minimal to no acute toxicity on D. magna. Which toxicant that did contribute to the toxicity 
in the bioplastic leachates is still unclear, as the toxicity identification evaluation results were unclear. 
The toxicant identified in the shopping bag, which may contribute to the acute toxicity on D. magna, 
could be metal and organic chemicals. In contrast, the toxicant that may contribute to chronic toxicity 
remains unclear, as no TIEs were applied, so the microplastics, metals, and organic chemicals could 
contribute to the toxicity. The toxicity in the leachate reduced the survival of parent D. magna, caused 
a delay in molting, a higher average cumulative offspring per parent in all concentrations, and a 
reduced average length of offspring in the 6.25 and 25 % leachates. 
The toxicant identified in the bio-waste bag, which may have contributed to the acute toxicity on D. 
magna, is unclear, but it could be either organic chemicals or microplastics. Moreover, the toxicant 
that may have contributed to chronic toxicity also remains unclear. The conclusion of the chronic 
toxicity of the shopping bag also applies to the bio-waste bag. Furthermore, the toxicity in the leachate 
reduced the survival of parent D. magna, caused a delay in molting, and a reduced average length of 
offspring in the 25 % leachates. 
Based on the obtained knowledge from the searched scientific literature, the challenges of bioplastics 
are: 
• they do not biodegrade because of ambient environmental conditions 
• they may contribute to microplastic debris on par with conventional plastics 
• the adverse effects of bioplastics can intensify when they coexist with other environmental 
pollutants when they adsorb onto bioplastics 
==PAGE:77==
 
77
• the littering becomes a more substantial problem due to the shifting of responsibilities from 
the consumers thinking that the bioplastic will disappear in nature 
• the consumers do not sort and recycle bioplastic correctly 
• the exact chemical compositions in bioplastics are unknown due to the opaque of what they 
contain 
Little is still known about the fate and behavior of the bioplastic in freshwater environments, as well 
as other environmental compartments. Also, more is needed to know about the toxicity that bioplastic 
leachates may pose to aquatic environments, as the studies addressing the effects of bioplastic 
leachates on freshwater species are limited. Therefore, further efforts are necessary to clarify the 
extent of the influence of the additives leached from bioplastics.

A.2 Acute toxicity test 1 - Acute immobilization test 
The acute toxicity test was conducted from January 16th to January 26th, 2024. Five (bio)plastics were 
used and are described in section 3.1. The method is described in section 3.2. Each (bio)plastic 
leachate had five test concentrations of 100, 50, 25, 12.5, and 6.25 %, with four replicates for each 
concentration percentage. The (bio)plastics were shaken in Milli-Q water for ten days. Due to the few 
offspring of D. magna per day, this acute toxicity test and the acute toxicity test with TIEs in 
Appendix A.3 were split into five parts over three weeks. The acute toxicity tests for all (bio)plastics 
were performed on these dates (the time start when adding D. magna in beakers):

A.3 Acute toxicity test 1 - Acute immobilization test combined with TIEs 
The acute toxicity test was conducted from January 16th to February 1st, 2024. Five (bio)plastics were 
used and are described in section 3.1. The method used is described in sections 3.2 and 3.2.5-3.2.6. 
Each (bio)plastic leachate was treated with activated carbon or EDTA. Unfiltered leachates were also 
tested. The test concentration percentage of the TIE-treated leachates was 25. Each TIE-treated 
leachate has four replicates. The (bio)plastics were shaken in Milli-Q water for ten days. Due to the 
few offspring of D. magna per day, this acute toxicity test combined with TIEs and the ordinary acute 
toxicity test in Appendix A.2 were split into five parts over three weeks. Controls for each TIE follow

Appendix B - Raw data for the second acute toxicity test 
B.1 Acute toxicity test 2 - Acute immobilization test 
The acute toxicity test was conducted from February 6th to February 22nd, 2024. Five (bio)plastics 
were used and are described in section 3.1. The method is described in section 3.2. Each (bio)plastic 
leachate had five test concentrations of 100, 50, 25, 12.5, and 6.25 %, with four replicates for each 
concentration percentage. The (bio)plastics were shaken in Milli-Q water for 20 days. Due to the few 
offspring of D. magna per day, this acute toxicity test and the acute toxicity test with TIEs in 
Appendix B.2 were split into four parts over three weeks. The acute toxicity tests for all (bio)plastics 
were performed on these dates (the time start when adding D. magna in beakers):

B.2 Acute toxicity test 2 - Acute immobilization test combined with TIEs 
The acute toxicity test was conducted from February 6th to February 22nd, 2024. Five (bio)plastics 
were used and are described in section 3.1. The method is described in sections 3.2 and 3.2.5-3.2.6. 
Each (bio)plastic leachate was treated with activated carbon or EDTA. Unfiltered leachates were also 
tested. The test concentration percentage of the TIE-treated leachates was 25, and 100 % unfiltered 
leachates were also used in this test. Each TIE-treated leachate has four replicates. The (bio)plastics 
were shaken in Milli-Q water for 20 days. Two extra controls with activated carbon and EDTA were 
also carried out, where they were made in the M7 media and with four replicates. 
Due to the few offspring of D. magna per day, this acute toxicity test combined with TIEs and the 
ordinary acute toxicity test in Appendix B.1 were split into four parts over three weeks. Controls for 
each TIE follow the dates in Appendix B.1. The acute toxicity tests with TIEs for all (bio)plastics 
were performed on these dates (the time start when adding D. magna in beakers):

B.3 Acute toxicity test 2.2 - Extra acute immobilization test for the shopping bag and 
its EDTA-TIE 
Due to the bad outcome of the second acute toxicity test on the shopping bag leachate and its EDTA-
treated leachate, a new test was conducted from Marts 19th to Marts 21st, 2024. The shopping bag was 
shaken in Milli-Q water for 20 days. The method is the same as the last test (Appendix B.1 and B.2 
for the ordinary and EDTA-TIE tests, respectively). This time, the 100 % leachate was adjusted with 
acid, so the pH was raised to the same as the M7 media (approx. pH 8.2). The test concentration 
percentages were again 100, 50, 25, 12.5, and 6.25, with four replicates for each concentration 
percentage.

B.4 Extra control tests for activated carbon, EDTA, and Milli-Q water 
Additional acute toxicity tests were conducted to ensure that it was not the activated carbon, EDTA, 
or Milli-Q water that contained some of the toxic components in the acute toxicity tests. The 
procedure of the toxicity test and the production of leachates (without using the (bio)plastics) was the 
same as the toxicity test with TIE in sections 3.2 and 3.2.6. The tests were conducted between 
February 6th and Marts 21st, 2024. Each extra control had four replicates, and M7 solutions were 
added. The M7 control for each extra control follows the date in Appendix B.2 (Activated carbon and 
EDTA) and Appendix B.3 (Milli-Q water). 
Table 43: Extra controls with activated carbon and EDTA in Milli-Q water. A control with filtered Milli-Q water was also 
conducted. Four replicates were used for each control. The pH and O2 were measured at the start and the end of the test. 
Immobilized D. magna were counted at the time 24 and 48 hours. The control date for each (bio)plastic can be found at the bottom of 
this table, and they followed the controls in Appendix B.1, except for the filtered Milli-Q water control, which followed the control in 
Appendix B.3.

Appendix C - Raw data for the third acute toxicity test 
C.1 Acute toxicity test 3 - Acute immobilization test with methanol and dimethyl 
sulfoxide extraction 
The acute toxicity tests with methanol and dimethyl sulfoxide (DMSO) extraction were conducted 
from Marts 19th to April 5th, 2024. Three (bio)plastics were used and are described in section 3.1. The 
method is described in section 3.3. Each (bio)plastic leachate had seven test concentrations of 10, 5, 
2.5, 1.25, 0.625, 0.313, and 0.156 mg (bio)pl./mL, with five replicates for each concentration. A total 
volume of 20 mL was used in all test beakers. The (bio)plastics were extracted with methanol and 
DMSO with evaporation under a stream of nitrogen. Due to the few offspring of D. magna per day, 
this test, the toxicity test with EDTA TIE (Appendix C.2), and the DMSO control (Appendix C.3) 
were split into four parts over two weeks. The acute toxicity tests for all (bio)plastics were performed 
on these dates (the time start when adding D. magna in beakers):

C.2 Acute toxicity test 3 - Acute immobilization test with EDTA-TIE on (bio)plastic 
extracts and control of DMSO 
The acute toxicity test with EDTA-TIE on (bio)plastic extracts was conducted from Marts 19th to 
April 5th, 2024. Three (bio)plastics were used and are described in section 3.1. The method used is 
described in sections 3.3 and 3.2.6 under “Using EDTA to neutralize metals”. The test concentrations 
of 2.5 and 10 mg (bio)pl./mL were used. There were five and three replicates for 2.5 and 10 mg 
(bio)pl./mL, respectively. A total volume of 20 mL was used in all test beakers. Due to the few 
offspring of D. magna per day, this toxicity test with EDTA TIE, the ordinary toxicity test (Appendix 
C.1), and the DMSO control (Appendix C.3) were split into four parts over two weeks.

Appendix D - Raw data, one-way ANOVAs, and the subsequent post hoc 
tests for chronic toxicity test 
The chronic toxicity test was conducted from April 17th to May 8th, 2024. Two bioplastics were used 
and are described in section 3.1. The method used is described in section 3.4. Three test concentrations 
of 25, 12.5, and 6.25 % were applied in a series with one bioplastic, with ten replicates for each 
concentration percentage. The bioplastics were shaken in Milli-Q water for 20 days. The duration of 
the chronic test was 21 days. The chronic test for both bioplastics followed the schedule below, which 
describes when the test solutions were changed and when D. magna was fed.

D.4 Feeding D. magna during the test 
Food was provided in the form of concentrated algae (Pseudokirchneriella subcapitata). The algae 
were centrifugated and counted, so the ratio levels of organic carbon were kept between 0.1 and 0.2 
mg C/daphnia/day. 
The carbon content is calculated based on the following equation: 
Carbon concentration [mgC
mL ] = W [cells
mL ] · 1 · 10−8 mgC
cell

D.7 One-way ANOVAs and the subsequent post hoc tests 
One-way ANOVAs were conducted on the average cumulative offspring per parent D. magna, the 
average lengths of parent D. magna, and the average lengths of D. magna offspring for the shopping 
bag and the bio-waste bag leachates. 
The subsequent post hoc tests were conducted on the cumulative offspring per parent D. magna for 
the shopping bag leachate and the average lengths of D. magna offspring for the shopping bag and 
the bio-waste bag leachates.

water) 
5 mL solution 1 
5 mL solution 2 
25 mL solution 3

- 
14.298 g H3BO3 
-

-

-

-

-

-

-

-

F.2 Carbon and nitrogen analyses of (bio)plastic leachates 
The unfiltered and filtered 10- and 20-day shaken (bio)plastic leachates were analyzed for carbon and 
nitrogen content on January 31st, 2024. The total organic and inorganic carbon together equals the 
total carbon, so if <TC and <QL are shown in the TOC and IC, respectively, only organic carbon was 
found, and the inorganic carbon was under the detection limit. In total nitrogen, if a number has a < 
in front, the nitrogen concentration was too low to be detected or was not present in the (bio)plastic 
leachate. The 10-day and the 20-day shaken leachates were analyzed twice and once, respectively.

F.3 Non-targeted analysis of (bio)plastic leachates 
The filtered 10-day shaken (bio)plastic leachates were analyzed for chemical features on January 31st, 
2024. The leachates were filtered in the 0.22-micron filter. The liquid chromatography-mass 
spectrometry (LC-MS) was applied. The following eluents were applied: Eluent A - Water with 0.1% 
formic acid and Eluent B - Methanol with 0.1% formic acid. Positive and negative ionization modes 
were utilized. The following data was processed on the MetaboScape Platform. DTU Food performed 
the analysis, and data was processed and provided to us.

Appendix G - Metal analyses of (bio)plastic extracts 
The (bio)plastic extracts were analyzed for metals on Marts 21st and 22nd, 2024. The ICP-MS and 
ICP-OES were applied. If a number has a < in front, the metal concentration was too low to be 
detected or not present in the (bio)plastic extract. The blank extract was also treated with methanol 
and DMSO. Three replicates were made on all (bio)plastics and the blank extracts.

Appendix H - EC50/LC50 data for metals on D. magna 
All acute toxicity tests were in the form of LC50 and EC50 for the species D. magna. All data was 
from the ECOTOX database (https://cfpub.epa.gov/ecotox/search.cfm), U.S. EPA database (GLI 
Clearinghouse MS Access), Biesinger & Christensen (1972), Khangarot & Ray (1989), and Okamoto 
et al. (2014). The effect concentration values were searched on the ECOTOX and U.S. EPA databases 
on February-May, 2024. The CAS numbers for the metals were found at https://chem.echa.europa. 
eu. The boxes with the same color within each metal represented data from the same study.

Appendix I - R.studios script for calculations of the effect concentrations 
ECX and the concentration-response curves 
The script was taken from course 12237, “Chemicals in the Environment”.

157
 
 
 
Appendix J - Python scripts for EC50/LC50 data for metals on D. magna 
J.1 Graph of EC50/LC50 data for metals on D. magna (the highest and the lowest found 
effect concentrations) 
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
 
metals = ["Na", "Mg", "K", "Ca", "V", "Cr", "Mn", "Fe", "Co", "Ni", "Cu", "Zn", 
"As", "Se", "Sr", "Nb", "Mo", "Cd", "Sn", "Hf", "W"] 
 
EC50 = [[402600, 1820000], [140000, 343560], [93000, 340000], [52000, 870000],  
==PAGE:158==
 
158
        [1200, 1580], [130, 1790], [2000, 156000], [2300, 9600], [720, 7370],  
        [510, 7590], [2, 1524], [68, 4700], [2400, 7400], [350, 2020],  
        [94000, 227070], 140, [203200, 3220000], [3.6, 1880], [6200, 55000],  
        9700, [30000, 893900]] 
 
fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
bar_width = 0.5 
 
for i, EC50_value in enumerate(EC50): 
    if isinstance(EC50_value, list): 
        low, high = EC50_value 
        ax.bar(i, high - low, bottom = low, width = bar_width, color = 'DarkBlue') 
    else: 
        ax.plot(i, EC50_value, 'o', color = 'DarkBlue') 
ax.set_xticks(range(len(metals))) 
ax.set_xticklabels(metals) 
plt.xticks(rotation = 'vertical') 
 
ax.set_yscale('log') 
ax.set_ylabel('Concentration [µg/L]') 
ax.set_xlabel('Metals') 
plt.show() 
 
J.2 Lowest found EC50/LC50-values of metals on D. magna and metal concentrations 
in unfiltered (bio)plastic leachates 
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
 
metals = ["Na", "Mg", "K", "Ca", "V", "Cr", "Mn", "Fe", "Co", "Ni", "Cu", "Zn", 
"As", "Se", "Sr", "Nb", "Mo", "Cd", "Sn", "Hf", "W"] 
 
EC50 = [402600, 140000, 93000, 52000, 1200, 130, 2000, 2300, 720, 510, 2, 68,  
        2400, 350, 94000, 140, 203200, 3.6, 6200, 9700, 30000] 
 
Shopping_bag_UF = [510, 550, 0, 7720, 1.35, 3.56, 7.32, 6.1, 0.2, 5.49, 0,  
                   44, 0, 0, 25.8, 0, 0.24, 0, 1.29, 0, 0] 
 
Food_waste_bag_UF = [350, 320, 0, 3510, 1.83, 0.46, 0.79, 5.60, 0, 0.53, 0,  
                     8, 0, 0, 5.3, 0.4, 0.34, 0, 0.44, 0, 0.15] 
 
Bio_waste_bag_UF = [0, 0, 0, 1180, 1.60, 0.18, 0.59, 4.4, 0, 0.66, 0, 16, 0,  
                    0, 8.1, 0, 0.26, 0, 0.22, 0, 0.56] 
 
Conventional_waste_bag_UF = [0, 130, 0, 1540, 1.48, 0.55, 0.74, 2.00, 0, 1.3,  
                             0, 25, 0, 0, 5.6, 0, 0.59, 0, 0, 0, 0] 
 
Grocery_carrier_bag_UF = [380, 0, 0, 3050, 1.57, 0.19, 2.71, 3.1, 0, 1.05, 0,  
                          23, 0, 0, 8.9, 0, 60.81, 0, 0, 0, 71.21] 
 
metals_to_plot = [] 
==PAGE:159==
 
159
EC50_to_plot = [] 
Shopping_bag_UF_to_plot = [] 
Food_waste_bag_UF_to_plot = [] 
Bio_waste_bag_UF_to_plot = [] 
Conventional_waste_bag_UF_to_plot = [] 
Grocery_carrier_bag_UF_to_plot = [] 
for i in range(len(metals)): 
    if Shopping_bag_UF[i] != 0 or Food_waste_bag_UF[i] != 0 or Bio_waste_bag_UF[i] 
!=0 or Conventional_waste_bag_UF[i] != 0 or Grocery_carrier_bag_UF[i]: 
        metals_to_plot.append(metals[i]) 
        EC50_to_plot.append(EC50[i]) 
        Shopping_bag_UF_to_plot.append(Shopping_bag_UF[i]) 
        Food_waste_bag_UF_to_plot.append(Food_waste_bag_UF[i]) 
        Bio_waste_bag_UF_to_plot.append(Bio_waste_bag_UF[i]) 
        Conventional_waste_bag_UF_to_plot.append(Conventional_waste_bag_UF[i]) 
        Grocery_carrier_bag_UF_to_plot.append(Grocery_carrier_bag_UF[i]) 
 
fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
bar_width = 0.17 
 
ax.plot(EC50[0], label = "Lowest EC50 values of metals", color = "red") 
for i in range(len(EC50_to_plot)): 
    if EC50_to_plot[i] != 0: 
        ax.plot([i, i + bar_width * 4], [EC50_to_plot[i], EC50_to_plot[i]], color 
='red') 
 
ax.bar([i + 0 * bar_width for i in range(len(Shopping_bag_UF_to_plot))], 
       Shopping_bag_UF_to_plot, width = bar_width, label = 'Shopping bag', 
       color = 'tab:blue') 
 
ax.bar([i + 1 * bar_width for i in range(len(Food_waste_bag_UF_to_plot))], 
       Food_waste_bag_UF_to_plot, width = bar_width, label = 'Food-waste bag', 
       color = 'orange') 
 
ax.bar([i + 2 * bar_width for i in range(len(Bio_waste_bag_UF_to_plot))], 
       Bio_waste_bag_UF_to_plot, width = bar_width, label = 'Bio-waste bag', 
       color = 'tab:green')

k <- 4; n <- 31 #Number of groups k, total number of observations n 
qf(p = 0.95, df1 = k-1, df2 = n - k) 
 
#### Post hoc 
(SST <- sum((a - mean(a))^2)) #Total variation, SST 
 
a1 <- a[1:6]; a2 <- a[7:15]; a3 <- a[16:22]; a4 <- a[23:31] #Residual variance 
after model fit, SSE 
(SSE <- sum((a1 - mean(a1))^2) + sum((a2 - mean(a2))^2) + sum((a3 - mean(a3))^2) 
+ sum((a4 - mean(a4))^2)) 
 
(SSTr <- SST - SSE) #Variance explained by the model, SS(Tr) 
 
(Fobs <- (SSTr/(k-1))/(SSE/(n-k))) #Test statistic 
 
(1 - pf(Fobs, df1 = k-1, df2 = n-k)) #P-value 
 
(muis <- tapply(a, aa, mean)) 
(mu <- mean(a)) 
(alpha <- muis - mu) 
 
muis[1] - muis[2] + c(-1, 1) * 
  qt(0.975, df = n - k) * sqrt(SSE/(n - k) * (1/6 + 1/9))

165
(tobs <- (muis[1] - muis[2])/sqrt(SSE/(n - k) * (1/6 + 1/9))) 
2 * (1 - pt(abs(tobs), n-k)) 
 
muis[1] - muis[3] + c(-1, 1) * 
  qt(0.975, df = n - k) * sqrt(SSE/(n - k) * (1/6 + 1/7)) 
(tobs <- (muis[1] - muis[3])/sqrt(SSE/(n - k) * (1/6 + 1/7)))

Appendix L - Workplace assessment (dan: arbejdspladsvurdering) 
 
 
Project information -  LAB  
General information: (*) 
DTU ENV project no: 
 
Project name: 
Ecotoxicity of bioplastic leachates to Daphnia magna 
Project Leader: 
Nanna B. Hartmann 
Project period: 
2/1/24 - 21/6/24 
Description of the project 
WHY 
The subject of this thesis is ecotoxicity of bioplastic leachates to freshwater 
plankton Daphnia magna. Although bioplastics are considered sustainable in 
terms of CO2 footprint and are safe to the environment, chemical additives and 
other components in bioplastics can be toxic to nature and organisms. 
Therefore, it is important to figure out the bioplastics and thereafter regulate 
through legislations. 
HOW 
In this thesis, biobased biodegradable plastic is used in natural freshwater 
medium and inoculum. The bioplastic is cut into small pieces and will leach into 
water where the effects will be tested on Daphnia magna at different 
concentrations of bioplastic leachate in the test media. From result, a 
concentration response curve can give information about e.g. the EC50. A test 
will be carried out with a positive control (potassium dichromate) to test the 
sensitivity of the test organism. 
WHAT 
Concerning the Daphnia magna toxicity test, it will be evident if some biobased 
biodegradable plastics have effects on freshwater plankton in the environment.
